==PAGE:2==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
Abstract
This project studies the feasibility of simulating multi-stage deep drawing of a part with
rectangular bottom using LS-DYNA. Using a specific process from the industrial part-
ner Steel Products, Aage Østergaard as the basis for this study, the simulation results
are evaluated based on how accurately they estimate the production samples, and which
parameters are most important for attaining sufficient accuracy for it to be a useful tool
for facilitating future process run-ins. Through the simulation process, methodologies are
developed for facilitating future use. Three of the four stages of the process are success-
fully simulated, and the last stage is almost fully simulated. Throughout the simulation
process, a multitude of difficulties are discovered, and methodologies for overcoming these
are proposed.
The overall conclusion is that, while it is possible to simulate the specific process studied
here to a sufficiently high degree of accuracy, it is not suitable as an initial step in trying
to implement LS-DYNA simulation into production. It could however be useful for pro-
cesses with fewer challenging aspects, or at a later point at which significant experience
using LS-DYNA to simulate similar processes has been accumulated, and with a more
suitable simulation environment.
I
==PAGE:3==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
Preface
This project, named “Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing of part with rectangular
bottom”, concerns modelling of industrial sheet metal forming, attempting to change and
optimize the current approach to tooling geometry prediction.
This thesis has been developed in order to fulfilment parts of the requirements of a mas-
ter’s degree from the Materials and Manufacturing Engineering program at the Technical
University of Denmark.
The work presented in this thesis took place between February and August of 2022,
and represents 30 ECTS.
The inspiration for this project is based on challenges faced by industrial partners of
DTU, namely Steel Products, Aage Østergaard, through their contact with supervisor
C.V. Nielsen. The project is in collaboration with Steel Products, Aage Østergaard, lo-
cated in Jutland, providing both information access to their production, information and
files related to specific products, as well as samples for testing.

1
Introduction
When a company or a manufacturing department is tasked with manufacturing a product
or component, requiring metal forming, in most cases, only the final geometry is provided.
Many such orders are not of a sufficiently large size required for justifying investing the
required resources for being able to simulate the forming processes to a sufficient accuracy.
With the exception of some full scale mass production, the current approach is to rely
on experienced toolmakers to be able to make what is essentially a qualified guess at
required tool geometries and processes, followed by physical testing and modification of
these. This involves significant economic risk, as the time and resource requirement de-
pends on the accuracy of the prediction and what is required to modify these. In the case
of the specific process modelled here, tooling required more than 40 iterations over the
course of approximately half a year.
The ability to simulate mechanical processes is resource intensive, and acquiring it in-
volves significant economic risk.
The first aspect is that the licences for the relevant
software are exorbitantly expensive. The second is that the skills required to efficiently
use the software is hard to come by.
Currently it would require having an employee
dedicated to full time simulation. The aim here is to lower the threshold for achieving
sufficient accuracy, to make the technology more accessible to production companies.
This project will focus on trying to simulate metal forming of a rectangular component
formed using multiple steps and specific processes. The simulations will be compared to
the physical results of the process. By changing the simulation geometries and parame-
ters, their effects on accuracy can be determined, as well as the effect on simulation time
required, and time required to build the simulation.
1.1
Process
The process has six total steps, of which four will be the focus, as the main geometry is
finished at that point, with the last two processes removing excess material and adding
details to the side of the walls. These last two steps do not significantly alter the geometry,
and do not cause problems for the production. A flowchart of the process steps is included
below in Figure 1.
1 of 125
==PAGE:8==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
Figure 1: Chart showing the general changes to the workpiece between the steps, excluding
the last two steps.
1.1.1
Material and blank
The use of the finalized component requires that the material does have some strength,
but since the process involves deep-drawing of a very thin sheet compared to the depth
of the deep drawing, the material needs to have a high formability. After trying multiple
materials the chosen one is EN 573-3 AW-8006. This is a wrought aluminium alloy con-
taining 1.2-2% Fe, as well as smaller amounts of Si, Cu, Mn, Mg, and Zn, in order to add
strength. The full datasheet can be found in Appendix 8
It is rolled to a thickness of 1mm and supplied in coils.
Due to the softness of the
material, no coil set compensation is done after de-coiling the sheet. The final blank
shape is cut using a laser-cutter. Since significant changes can be seen when using dif-
ferent parts of the coil, the coil used in the production is taken from the center part of
a larger master coil. The supplier creates this master coil, and the production company
has simply specified that they would only like to receive coils cut from the center of the
master. When exactly this was done, if it will have an effect on any tests results, or what
exactly the effects were, is not known.
1.1.2
Step 1
The first step is deep-drawing. The initial blank has an approximately oval shape, which
reflects the final shape. The blank is held in place by a system of pins along the outer
edge. These do not have any other effect on the process than ensuring correct positioning
of the blank. The rolling direction is parallel to the long radius of the blank. The punch
has two roundings on the edge dividing the 90° turn into one 65° turn with a radius
r = 10.2mm, followed by a 25° rounding at r = 15.2mm, reducing the punch diameters
from D = 622.5mm, d = 478.5mm to D = 521.35mm, d = 378mm. A visualization of the
all the punch edge profiles, can be seen on Figure 2.
2 of 125
==PAGE:9==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
Figure 2: Punch edge profiles for all relevant steps.
The punch travel is 200mm, which is slightly more than is required to fully eliminate
the flange. The gap between blankholder and die is set to 1.18mm. This gap is fixed in
the production setup, but due to tool deflection caused by having step 1 and 2 on the
same press, this gap may vary. To remedy this, the blankholder is force controlled. By
setting the force to be sufficiently high to stop any movement, the gap can be controlled
physically by inserting shims in the gap. The blankholder force is set to 2500kN for the
full press, encompassing step 1 and 2, and production estimates an approximate equal
distribution between the two, making the step 1 blankholder force 1250kN.
The production setup and the product of this step is shown below on Figures 3a and
3b.
(a) Production setup showing the die and punch
including the product of step 1.
(b) Production setup showing the blankholder
Figure 3: Step 1.
1.1.3
Step 2
The second step involves redrawing of the product from step 1. The aim here is to grad-
ually make the cup more rectangular. The die geometry resembles that of the step 1
3 of 125
==PAGE:10==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
punch. While significant springback occurs after step 1, the formability allows for putting
the blank on top of the die, and simply letting the closing of the machine push the blank
down over the die. The blankholder takes the shape of the die, being a countershape,
scaled up to ensure a constant gap of 1.2mm between die, blankholder, and punch. The
punch comes up from the center of the die, pulling the blank through the gap. Illustrations
of this is included in later sections discussing this. This punch has stepped edge roundings
similar to the step 1 punch to prevent stress concentrations in the radius regions. The
gap is controlled in the same manner as the step 1 gap, considering that they are in the
same press. While the CAD specifies a punch travel resulting in no flange, the physical
production samples have a slight rounded flange resulting from the blank not being fully
pulled through the blankholder-die gap.
The production setup and the product of this step is shown below on Figures 4a, 4b,
and 4c respectively.
(a) Production setup showing the punch and
die.
(b) Production setup showing the blankholder.
(c) Sample product from step 1.
Figure 4: Step 2.

August 5, 2022

1.1.4
Step 3
The third step is a further redrawing again making the cup more rectangular. This step
differs from step 2 by having slightly different roundings on the punch, and by varying
the gap between punch and die, having a gap of 1.2mm on the sides, and 1.35mm around
the corners. Furthermore steps 3-6 are all performed on one press, with only step 3 and 4
using a blankholder. The blankholder force is 1000kN distributed between the two steps.
This distribution is estimated to be approximately equal. The nominal blankholder gap
is decreased to 1mm according to the provided CAD. A significant flange is present after
the process, indicating that the edge thickness has increased too far beyond the gap size.
The production setup and the product of this step is shown in Figures 5a and 5b.
(a) Production setup.
(b) Sample product from step 1.
Figure 5: Step 3.
1.1.5
Step 4
The fourth step is similar to step 3, further making the cup more rectangular, only vary-
ing in that the punch rounding no longer being stepped, but rather having a single large
rounding of r = 26.781mm, as measured on the production CAD.
The production setup can be seen behind step 3 on Figure 5a and the product of this step
is shown below on Figure 6.
5 of 125

Figure 6: Final geometry after step 4.
1.1.6
Step 5-6
In agreement with the industrial partner, the two final steps are not considered relevant
for simulation studies. These two steps do not present significant challenges during pro-
duction, and would require different types of simulation models. In step 5 groves are
added to the sides for increased structural integrity. In step 6 these groves are further
refined and the remaining flange is cut at the end of the process.

1.2
Project scope
The aim of this work is to attain first the the simulation results of the specific process.
Secondly the effect of different factors are determined in regards to accuracy of the sim-
ulation results and computation time. Thirdly an effective methodology is needed for
decreasing the practical time required to set up and run sufficiently accurate simulations.
Since the results of step 2-4 is dependent on the results of the previous step, ascertaining
sufficient accuracy with acceptable computation times, as well as establishing an effective
methodology, is based on the first step of the process. Initially a simplified version of step
1 is used for establishing a model which can run without errors and finishing in outputting
a sufficiently realistic result. This model is then used with the true geometry. Since the
process is on the limit of what is doable both physically and digitally, the outer bounds
of simulation parameters which can produce a blank of the approximately desired shape
is found, in regards to which physical phenomenon can be included in the simulations,
which keywords should be used, their settings, mesh sizes and geometries, and variable
ranges, such as friction coefficients and material strength. With these bounds established,
it is determined which exact settings result in sufficient accuracy in the smallest possible
timeframe.
Using the resulting geometry and model, the second step is simulated, and the results
of this is used to adapt the model until a sufficiently accurate resulting geometry is found.
This is repeated for the third and fourth step.
The resulting geometries of all steps
are compared to measurements of the production parts, in order to ascertain where de-
viations occur, and what effects they have on following steps, as well what might be the
cause as how these deviations could be prevented.
2
Sheet metal forming
2.1
Deep drawing
Deep-drawing traditionally refers to a process of metal forming where a punch, draw-
ing die, and commonly a blankholder, creates hollow three-dimensional parts from sheet
metal. Deep-drawing is very common, being used for machine components, like parts
for the automotive industry, and consumer products from sinks to cans. Conventional
deep-drawing is what is used for the the first step of process being studied here. Most of
the theoretical calculations are based on round axisymmetric parts. Furthermore, since a
non-axisymmetric geometry would cause independent parameters to no longer be inter-
dependent, making a full analytical solution impossible. Despite this being a rectangular
cup, these need to be included as a foundation[1].
For cylindrical cups the drawing ratio is expressed as equation (1)[1].
β = D0
dp
(1)
D0 being the blank diameter and dp the punch diameter. Assuming constant sheet thick-
ness t = t0 and the thickness being much lower than the punch diameter t0 << dp, the
8 of 125

(2)
Large drawing ratios risk bottom fracture, as the increased wall height facilitates necking
near the punch edge, as this region will experience tensile stress for the entirety of the
drawing. For most materials this limit is in the range of β < 1.6 −2.2[2]. For aluminium
alloys this would be in the lower end of this range, as it has poor strain distribution
characteristics[1].
If one were to approximate the ratio of step 1 by assuming it as a round cup, the ra-
tio would be (3)[1].
β =
p
dp(dp + 4h)

545.77mm(545.77mm + 4 · 175mm)

(3)
Where the values used are based on the radius of a circle of equal area to the true oval
shape. Using the average diameter between major and minor results in approximately the
same β = 1.507. This is just on the approximate 1.6 limit for aluminium alloys. Together
with the higher local stresses from not being axisymmetric, this means that this process
is on the limit of what is feasible[1].
It should be noted, that all dimensions used here are based on measurements made in the
production CAD model.
The drawing in causes compressive hoop stresses in the circumferential direction. For
the flange, this can cause it to buckle. This is why a blankholder is normally applied
to prevent buckling, or as it is often referred to, flange wrinkling or folding. Due to the
compressive circumferential stresses, an instability phenomenon might occur, similar to
Euler buckling in beams. This buckling behaviour can be prevented or limited by apply-
ing force normal to the flange surface. The blankholder is often forced controlled, as the
need to prevent wrinkling is constant as the flange changes in thickness. The expression
for this is shown in equation 4[1].
pN = (0.002...0.003)((β −1)2 +
dp
200t0
)Rm
(4)
Rm is here the fracture strength of the sheet material. This is known from tensile tests
to be Rm =≈109MPa. The tensile testing and its results can be found in Section 4.1.2.
In step 1, with the previous assumptions, the pressure range can be calculated as 5[1].
PN = (0.002...0.003)((1.511 −1)2 + 545.77mm
200 · 1mm)109MPa = (0.652...0.978)MPa
(5)
Considering that the blankholder area is 0.512m2, this equates to 334.02kN −501.03kN.
This is lower than the 125t or 1226.25kN specified by the production. It should however
be kept in mind that the calculations here are based on axisymmetric cups, which expe-
rience significantly more homogeneous stress concentrations than the oval or rectangular
parts studied here.

to become thicker, as the uniaxial compression becomes significantly higher in this part.

(7)
This is slightly lower than the production gap. According to the production CAD, the
fixed gap is 1.18mm.
A larger die corner radius rD lowers the drawing load, but it also lowers the blankolder
area, increasing the probability of wrinkling occurring in the die radius region. The die
radius can be estimated using an empirical equation (8).
rD = 0.035(50 + (D0 −dp))√t0[mm] = 0.035(50 + 262)

(8)
The production corner radius is constant at 10mm, so it can be assumed that there was
a significant need to lower the drawing load[1].
2.2
Redrawing
Conventional deep-drawing is a single-step process, but if the drawing ratio is too high,
successive drawing can be useful. One of these successive drawing methods is redrawing.
This is commonly done as reverse drawing by decreasing the radius and turning the
workpiece inside out, but can also be done as it is for this process. Having the total bend
change between steps be as low as possible increases the maximum achievable wall height,
and having it be less than 90° decreases the drawing load, but requires a new blankholder
geometry to prevent wrinkling[1].
2.3
Stress and strain in deep- and re-drawing
The process is qualitatively described by five sub-regions.
1. Radial drawing-in of the material between the drawing die and blankholder.
2. Bending and unbending of the material over the die curvature.
3. Stretching of the material in the cup wall between the punch and the die.
4. Stretching of the material over the punch rounding.
5. Stretching of the material at the bottom of the cup.
These are illustrated on Figure 9.
10 of 125
==PAGE:17==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
Figure 9: Sub-regions in deep drawing[1].
Sub-process (1) occurs in zone A-B. As the flange is pulled in, in the radial direction, the
radial stress increases, as well as the hoop stresses. In this zone the blankholder provides
significant compressive stresses. This influence is removed as the flange transitions to the
B-C zone, which is why, as previously mentioned, having an excessive die rounding radius
can cause wrinkles. This is somewhat counteracted by the normal pressure caused by
the tensile stress caused by the drawing. Sub-process (2) occur in the B-C zone. Here
the radial stress increases due to the further circumferential reduction, and due to the
friction caused by the normal force. Through this zone, the circumferential stress changes
from compressive to tensile. Sub-process (3) occurs in the C-D zone. Here the material
undergoes uniaxial tension, incurring stress is in mainly the r direction, but also in the
θ directions.
Sub-process (4) occurs in the D-E zone, and mainly experiences biaxial
stretching and bending over the nose. Almost the same is the case for sub-process (5) in
the E-F zone, though transitioning to balanced biaxial stretching[1].
The resulting geometry can be differed from where different stress components occur.
In the center and on the edge of the punch rounding, the material experiences constant
tensile stress, causing the local thickness to be the smallest, referred to as necking, and is
also why bottom fracture is one of the most common failure mechanisms. The further out
radially, the longer it will experience radial compression, causing the edge of the flange
to attain the highest thickness[1].
Based on the calculations of the stresses and their expressions, the forces can be found.

2.4
Friction and lubrication
Since the processes which this project focuses on all involves moving surfaces in contact
with each other, the friction and lubrication is extremely important. Since friction is a
complex phenomenon there are many mechanisms at work during these processes, many
of which will not be able to be included in the simulations, thus becoming one of the main
issues which could be the cause of deviations between simulation results and production
samples. For this reason, a theoretical understand of the mechanisms is needed, which is
the focus of this section.
Friction is defined as the surface force acting perpendicular to the normal force. When
two surfaces are in contact friction provides resistance to sliding. When the force exceeds
the static friction slippage occurs and the sliding surfaces is instead subjected to dynamic
friction.
Since the different types of frictions occur at different conditions and with different mech-
anisms, they are often modelled separately as:
• Friction at rest, sticking or static friction.
• Friction at movement, sliding friction.
High speed friction, generally meaning speeds beyond which even intermittent static fric-
tion can occur, is most often modeled by the Coulomb friction model. The version used
in LS-DYNA is given by equation (13)[4].
12 of 125
==PAGE:19==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
FC = µ|FN|sgn(vrel)
(13)
FC = Friction force, µ = Friction coefficient, FN = Normal force, vrel = Velocity of relative
movement.
Low speed friction is modelling can include modelling of the Dahl effect, Stribeck effect,
and the transition from static to sliding friction. Dahl’s friction model ignores viscous
effects but instead models based on contact stress and relative displacement modelling
the friction caused by micro motions such as pre-sliding behaviour, constituting the point
at which static friction is not exceeded, but the force is sufficient to deform asperities at
the surface.[5] The Stribeck effect manifesting the non-linear dependence of friction on
sliding speed, which can have a massive effect, like for example with cast iron in contact
with nitrided surfaces having a friction coefficient varying from 0.03 to 0.1[6].
While the speed, and thus transition, is highly important, modelling most of these ef-
fects would require significant computing power, making them unsuitable for dynamic
FEM. Not modelling many of these can cause multiple problems. If a model only applies
Coulomb friction, at very low speeds, such as the starting point of a linearly increasing
static load, the friction force will be sufficient to counter any movement. Considering it
as a passive force means it wont make the acceleration negative, but it extends the static
friction contrary to physical laws[7].
The friction modelling used here is mainly utilizing the Coulomb model, meaning that
the coefficient of friction is needed. Normally for metal on metal calculations this is es-
timated as being µ = 0.1. This is typically validated by comparing force curves, formed
geometries, or a friction study. This value is thus the basis of all models where a change
in coefficient is specified.
In metal forming processes the friction is heavily dependent on the lubrication, and lu-
brication is thus essential in most of these processes. The lubricant film separates the
workpiece and tool surfaces and also acts to cools the tool and workpiece. Separating the
surfaces with a film of lubrication is essential to facilitating deformation, lowering tool
loads, reducing wear, and avoiding several negatives effects of direct contact, such as pick-
up of material to the tool surfaces, as well as heat transfer. In metal forming however, the
amount of lubricant is rarely sufficient to have any significant effect on temperature. Since
actually separating the surfaces is often not possible in metal forming, extreme-pressure
additives are added to facilitate boundary layer lubrication.
In regards to metal forming, the basic mechanisms are:
• Hydrodynamic lubrication.
• Mixed lubrication.
• Boundary lubrication.
• Solid-film lubrication.
These domains and their effects can be visualized using a modified Sribeck curve, as seen
on Figure 10.
13 of 125
==PAGE:20==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
Figure 10: Modified Stribeck Curve Showing lubrication regimes.[8]
Hydrodynamic lubrication
When the lubricant film is sufficiently thick to completely separate the asperities, and
the load is completely transferred through pressurized viscous film, the friction is signifi-
cantly lowered. This typically occurs when the film thickness is more than ten times the
composite roughness, defined in equation (14).
Rcomp =
q
R2
tool + R2
wp
(14)
Whether this is desirable or not is dependent on the geometry. If there is full separation,
then the workpiece does not take the exact shape of the tools. While this is an important
aspect for very small geometries, where it is not necessarily desirable, for large geometries
such as the ones studied here, this is highly desirable. This will often occur locally, but
in most processes the conditions are insufficient for complete hydrodynamic separation.
Mixed lubrication
This occurs when the average film thickness is slower, allowing some asperity contacts,
meaning that the load is partially carried by the film pressure, and partially by the
contacts. Since its only some of the asperities, very little pick-up occurs. This can occur
without pressurized lubrication, and is typically observed locally pressurized pockets in
the workpiece. This phenomenon is called microhydrodynamic lubrication[9].
Boundary lubrication
Here the load is completely carried by the asperities, but metal to metal is prevented by
a very thin film. This is often only a few molecular layers either physically absorbed or
14 of 125
==PAGE:21==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
chemisorbed on the metal surface. In order to ensure that metal to metal contact does
not occur, additives are often added which chemically bond to the surface to ensure a
minimum of boundary lubrication[9].
Solid film lubrication
Solid film lubricants is low shear strength agents applied in the interface to prevent contact
or to physically or chemically absorb lubricants to prevent removal[9].
2.4.1
Effect of topography on friction
Considering that the contact models use a constant friction across the entirety of the
contacts, local effects might cause disparities. Therefore, the effects of topography on
friction is important to understand[10].
The topology is a defining element in lubrication due to it allowing for the lubricant
to enter pockets on the surface, and thus remain on the surface for longer and in greater
amounts. While counterintuitive, this can result in rougher surfaces resulting in lower
friction [10].
One of the main factors is the viscosity of the lubricant.
For low viscosity lubricant
the mechanism will be microplasto hydrostatic lubrication, where as for high viscosity oil
the main mechanism is microplasto hydrodynamic lubrication. For medium viscosity a
combination of these occur. A combination of both occurring is usually the most desirable
effect, meaning that the viscosity should be chosen based on the effects of other factors.
The drawing speed starts affecting the friction at higher speeds, which will assist in film
formation, much like high viscosity. Increasing degree of reduction or die angle decreases
the lubrication pressure, and thus increases friction as well as lubricant loss over time.
Increasing back tension increases the pressure, but has the viscosity increases with pres-
sure, the normal force is decreased again lower the viscosity [10].
The effect of pockets on the surface is dependent on the size of the pockets as well
as the conditions. When the lubricant pockets enter the deformation zone, deformation
due to compression will decrease the volume, increasing the hydrostatic pressure. Since
compression of the contact surface is the main state during deep drawing, the effect of
pockets is increased. This however is not constant through the process, as the volume will
not decrease significantly with further reduction, and can increase if parts of the process
includes local stretching. In multi-step processes, the surface roughness will decrease,
meaning that this effect will wear off, and friction will increase[10].
In the case of rolled material or material annealed to the point of significant grain reori-
entation, channels along the surface can facilitate lubricant escape, increasing friction[10].
2.4.2
Friction modelling in FEM
Regular contact modelling only account for contact between bodies, meaning that the con-
tact surfaces are smooth and thus having no friction. The reason for this is that including
friction in the contact model causes the stiffness matrices to become asymmetrical. It
thus requires the use of algorithms which can restore symmetry. Utilizing these can result
15 of 125
==PAGE:22==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
in convergence problems if the frictional stresses or displacements become large[11].
Coulombs friction is the most commonly used friction model in contact problem sim-
ulations. As previously stated, this is not applicable for low speed contact, and also does
not model the use of lubrication. While the inaccuracy is a problem, in dynamic mod-
elling such as what is usually done in LS-DYNA, the magnitude of acceleration and force
is dependent on the friction, which can further exacerbate the problems. Usually this
is solved by constructing boundaries for the effect of friction, and this is also the case
in LS-DYNA. How the lower boundary behaviour is handled is not something users can
change, as it is integral to the simulation stability, but a static friction can be defined.
This is however not desirable as the transition can cause discontinuous motion, and large
changes in acceleration is generally to be avoided. Furthermore, the initial force of any
movement will not be accurate, and force before movement or deformation being of low
importance. An upper limit can be defined for simulation the shear stress of the material,
but the ultimate upper boundary is not clearly explained. The effect of this should in
theory not be a problem, since despite friction increasing with speed, at very high speeds
exceeding 10m
s , the friction factor will decrease significantly[4].
In LS-DYNA almost all contacts are modelled using the *CONTACT keywords. The
friction using this is defined by the coefficient of friction relative to sliding velocity. The
formulation of this is shown in equation (15).
µ = FD + (FS −FD) exp −DC|vrel|
(15)
FD, FS, and DC are parameters which modulate the relationship between speed and
friction coefficient. By setting FD = −2, defined curves can be used for the friction.
It should be noted that this is the change in friction relative to speed, as usually the
friction coefficient is the only input. The curves can also be defined as functions, such as
a function of contact pressure, which in combination with thickness offset, can be used to
create a model approximating a lubrication layer. Previous testing has determined that
the effect of the accuracy of the input coefficient is large, but that accuracy is high when
this is based on physical tests[4].
2.5
Material modelling
For modelling the materials used in this process, several models and methods of modelling
are used. These are described here, as well the the theoretical background.
2.5.1
Hardening
As all relevant material models require a stress strain curve, this is one of the most im-
portant material factors.
The most common mathematical expressions use the strength factor "C", a strain hard-
ening exponent "n", and the prestrain "B". These models are the Ludwig-Hollomon and
Swift stress strain curves.
Ludwig-Hollomon
σf = Cεn
eq
Swift
σf = C(B + εeq)n
16 of 125
==PAGE:23==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
One of the largest factors which influence the hardening exponent is the grain size, gen-
erally increasing with the average grain size.
Since this is metal forming of aluminium, the most fitting hardening law is the Voce
hardening law, which is based on the Swift hardening law. Voce hardening is what is used
in the final material model applied here. This is expressed by equation (16)[12][13].
σf = A −Be−Cεeq
(16)
The Voce law is an exponential law, with the constants determined from experimental
fitting.
2.5.2
Anisotropy
Anisotropy in sheet metal describes differences in flow stress depending on the mechanical
and crystallographic anisotropy. Mechanical anisotropy is caused by the grains elongat-
ing, This influences the ultimate tensile strength, usually being lower in the direction
perpendicular to the rolling direction. Crystallographic anisotropy is caused by the pre-
ferred orientation of the grains. This is expressed quantitatively by the anisotropy factor
R. This is often defined by tensile testing at different rolling directions. When performing
the tensile tests, the anisotropy factors are defined by equation (17)[1].
Rα = εb
εt
=
ln(b1
b0)
ln(t1
t0)
(17)
α is the rolling direction of the sample, εb is the width strain, and εt is the thickness strain
of the tensile test. The normal anisotropy can be found with the three directions 0°, 45°,
and 90°[1].
¯R = R0° + 2R45° + R90°
4
(18)
For FCC material like aluminium, the anisotropy constant will most commonly be a sine
curve topping at 0° and 90°, with values of approximately 1, and having its minimum at
45°[1].
The anisotropy of the sheet material has a significant effect on the formability. Larger
¯R will limit the drawing ratio.
In the case of deep-drawing of a circular cup, planar
anisotropy will lead to earing. The effect is shown on Figure 11[1].
Figure 11: Influence of planar anisotropy on earing in deep drawing[1].

==PAGE:24==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
As can be seen from Figure 11, the most likely earing behaviour will be more flange near
the 0° and 90° directions, as the material in question is an FCC aluminium alloy.
2.5.2.1
Plasticity for anisotropic sheet material
The yield criteria and flow rule are used to constitute models capable of describing large
strain deformation.
For isotropic materials, the commonly used yield criterion is von Mises (19).
(σxx −σyy)2 + (σyy −σzz)2 + (σxx −σzz)2 + 6(τ 2
xy + τ 2
yz + τ 2
zx) = 2σ2
f
(19)
Here σf is the yield stress in uniaxial tension or compression.
For anisotropic materials, a commonly used yield criterion is Hill’s shown in equation
(20).
H(σxx −σyy)2 + F(σyy −σzz)2 + G(σxx −σzz)2 + 2Nτ 2
xy + 2Lτ 2
yz + 2Mτ 2
zx = 1
(20)
In plane stress this simplifies to (21).
¯f =
r
1
2(Fσ2
y + Gσ2
x + H(σx −σy)2 + Nσ2
xy)
(21)
Here F, G, H, L, M, and N depend on the anisotropy conditions, and can be fitted to
experimental results. This is made such that it reduces to the von Mises criterion with
decreasing anisotropy. The variables can be determined from uniaxial stretch tests in the
principal directions.
From the derivation of Hill’s criterion, it can be determined that increasing the nor-
mal anisotropy implies greater principal stresses before yielding if the stresses have the
same sign, and smaller principal stresses before yielding if the signs are opposite. This
can be visualized as Figure 12
18 of 125
==PAGE:25==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
Figure 12:
Anisotropic yield ellipse depicted with the normal anisotropy R as a
parameter[1].
For deep-drawing, the stress in the wall will mainly be in one direction, meaning that
greater normal anisotropy results in more rapid fracture in the cup wall if yielding occurs.
However, it also means that less stress is needed to draw in the flange, as the drawing
stress is tensile, and the radial stress is compressive. In practice that means, that an in-
crease in normal anisotropy allows for greater loads in the flange, increasing the possible
cup height, as it allows for a larger flange. The stress in the wall is also not complete
one-directional, as there is positive stress resulting from the punch preventing the diam-
eter from decreasing when the wall is stretched. As this places the wall stress in the first
quadrant of the yield ellipse, the risk of breakage is reduced.
The relationship between the anisotropy and the equivalent strain, assuming plane stress,
can be expressed as equation (22).
εeq =
1 + R
√
1 + 2R
r
ε2

2R
1 + Rεxεy + ε2
y
(22)
Hill’s criterion is however not the only yield criterion which can be used to model anisotropy,
as it requires several assumptions, such as, identical material properties in all direction in
the plane, constant normal anisotropy during deformation, significantly smaller stresses in
the out of plane principal direction, no Bauschinger effect, and superimposed hydrostatic
pressure does not affect tendency to yield. Due to this, several other models have been
developed.
For anisotropic metals modelled using shell elements, LS-DYNA has 26 material mod-
els, some utilizing similar yield criteria, modified to handle different features. Excluding
those which are used to model effects which are not relevant here, like damage behaviour,
failure behaviour, surface effects, temperature dependence, thermal effects, and thick shell
19 of 125
==PAGE:26==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
elements, and excluding those which require input which is not attainable in this project,
the main models use versions of Hill’s criterion, and versions of Barlat’s yield criterion.
Barlat and Lian proposed a non-quadratic yield criterion introducing a coupling between
normal and sheer stress components, based on polycrystalline plasticity theory. The Bar-
lat and Lian yield criterion can be described by equation (23)[14].
¯f =
2k
r
1
2(a(K1 + H2)2k + a(K1 −K2)2k + (2 −a)(2K2)2k)
(23)

Here the constants "a", "h", and "p" are fitted to experimental results. The exponent
"2k" is defined by crystal structure of the given material[14].
Which of these two is the more accurate is difficult to ascertain, as it is mostly related to
the circumstances. For instance, one study found that the most accurate was dependent
on the calibration of the test results[14].
Barlat and his colleagues have since developed several models based on this, named by
the publication year, capable of modelling different phenomena, as well as combinations
of these. In this project, the model used is Barlat YLD2000.
Barlat YLD2000 is developed to model plane stress yield for anisotropic sheet metals.
The advantage of this model is that it is very accurate in modelling anisotropic plastic-
ity, by taking σ0°, σ45°, σ90°, R0°, R45°, R90°, and σb into account for plane stress, with
σb = σxx = σyy for equal biaxial stress alligned to the rolling direction, while optimizing it
for use in FE simulation. Furthermore, it was developed by applying it to FCC aluminium
alloys, as is the case here[15][16].
The full formulation of Barlat 2000 is not included here, as the LS-DYNA implemen-
tation automatically determines most of the parameters based on user-input from tensile
tests. It has also shown itself to be more accurate than using Hill’s criterion in similar
deep drawing simulations of aluminium parts of similar thicknesses[17][18][19].
The implementation is further explored in Section 4.3.6.1.
3
Numerical simulation by FEM
Finite element analysis (FEA), also called the finite element method (FEM), is a method
of finding numerical solutions to field problems, meaning to seek spacial distribution de-
pendent on one or more dependent variables. Most FEA programs are in a form requiring
only a very limited understanding of the methods applied, like LS-DYNA. However, not
having this understanding invites problems, which is why the relevant basics are included
here.
20 of 125
==PAGE:27==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
3.1
Discretization by finite number of elements
In order to create a mathematical model for continuous field, it needs to be discretized,
meaning that it is divided into a construction of finite elements, creating a piecewise
continuous field approximating the part of reality which is being modelled. Since this
is fundamentally an approximation, it introduces modelling and discretization errors[20].
This is also the case for the program in question LS-DYNA.
A simple example of this is the tapered support post. The physical discretized repre-
sentation of this could be a stack of boxes with increasing cross-sectional area from the
top down. By increasing the amount of total elements, the closer the approximation of
the taper would be. As it is under axial stress, the finite element representation could be
a set of one-dimensional beams of varying strength relative to the corresponding cross-
sectional area and overall material strength. For this approximation, usually polynomial
interpolation is utilized[20].
3.2
Element types
The main elements used here are linear triangles and bilinear quadrilaterals.
This is
because higher degree elements are not supported for LS-DYNA shell elements.
The
focus on shell elements is further explained in Section 4.3.2.
Linear triangle (CST)
Linear triangles are plane triangles with field quantities varying linearly with the cartesian
coordinates x and y in stress analysis. They are the simplest planar element to create and
solve. The linear displacement field results in a constant strain field, which is why they
can also be called constant-strain triangles (CST). These have 3 nodes per element, with
2 degrees of freedom per node, hence making it 6 degrees of freedom (DOF) per element.
The shape functions for these are (25)[20].

(42)

(43)
Where the deviatoric stress tensor ˜s’s indices "xx", "yy", and "xy", signify the stress com-
ponents, here only being in plane stress[22][21].
The only major downside to this is that it still fails to capture the Bauschinger effect,
where drastic reorganization of dislocation structures cause a drop of flow stress upon
stress reversal[22][21]. As the process in question is multi-step sheet metal forming, the
material will be subjected to cyclical loading conditions, making this downside a major
potential source of deviations[23].
3.3
Explicit or implicit solutions
Both of these methods are approaches to approximating solutions to time-dependent equa-
tions. The overall difference is that explicit methods calculate the future state from the
current state, and implicit methods find the current state using boundary conditions and
the previous state[24].
While the implicit methods are more computationally demanding, the accuracy of the
solution is higher, and in order to attain sufficient accuracy, the explicit methods would
require the time-steps to be lowered. Which methods will lead to the fastest and most
accurate solution is thus dependent on the model.
For similar accuracy, the explicit
method is typically faster, but encountering more difficulty as the necessity for accuracy
increases[24].
3.4
Quasi-static or dynamic formulations
The terms are often conflated as implicit using a quasi-static formulation and explicit
using a dynamic formulation. While they are somewhat tied, these are not the same in
theory.
Quasi-static means that the stiffness matrix and force vectors are time-independent, mean-
ing inertia is ignored. In practise this means that the magnitude of the speed of the process
is not considered, such that the same results would be obtained for an identical process set
to take place in 1ms or 1min. For quasi-static formulations, the time integration scheme
can both be explicit and explicit.
Dynamic formulations’ constitutive equations are elasto-plastic or viscoplastic, meaning
that the main structure is mass and dampening matrices and force vectors. The time
integration for this is always explicit[25].
If the simulation does not include time-dependent properties like temperature, and highly
speed dependent friction mechanisms, or at least is not heavily dependent on them, a
quasi-static formulation will be sufficient and less CPU intensive[25].
24 of 125
==PAGE:31==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing

It should be noted that in LS-DYNA, using the implicit method means that the model is
quasi-static, and using the explicit method means that the model is dynamic. A dynamic
model using the implicit model is possible, but only for specific springback analysis.
3.5
Contact formulations
The definition of contact in LS-DYNA is identifying the location of, nodes, parts, seg-
ments or sets of these, and if the slave, identifying if these have penetrated the master
segment. If penetration does occur, then several methods can be chosen of how to handle
this. Usually this is a penalty formulation, where a force is applied proportional to the
penetration depth. Simpler formulations are based on displacing the penetrating node or
part until it no longer penetrates. While less calculations are required for this method,
properly calculating friction is not possible, and it is significantly less robust[26].
The penalty formulation is most effective when applied to two non-rigid bodies, as the
forces are distributed between two parts, lowering force peaks, but it can also lead to nu-
merical instabilities, so it is often convenient and relatively effective to use it in contacts
25 of 125
==PAGE:32==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
with rigid bodies[26].
Contacts are mathematically represented by linear springs between nearest slave and
master nodes. The stiffness of the spring determines the force, and different formulations
scale the force differently as a function of penetration. While this formulation is the de-
fault, it breaks down for large differences between materials, as the materials are used to
determine the spring stiffness in the spring calculation. This is defines as the "SOFT =
0" option in *CONTACT[27].
The non-default option "SOFT = 1" calculates the stiffness of the spring based on nodal
masses as well as the global time step. It is thus completely independent from the mate-
rials. The stiffness is calculated as the nodal mass divided by the square of the time step
size, with a scaling factor added for stability. This option often results in significantly
higher stiffness, and is generally recommended for impact analysis[27].
The "SOFT = 2" option uses the same calculation as "SOFT = 1", but is based on
segment contact, applying the forces to 4-noded segments. This option increases stiffness
like "SOFT = 2", but is significantly more stable, as the chance of shooting nodes is sig-
nificantly lowered. This makes it suitable for models which are showing significant contact
problems. The term "shooting nodes" here refers to a common error in LS-DYNA, and
dynamic FEA simulations in general, where one or more nodes are mishandled in the
contact formulation, resulting in their velocities increasing far beyond what is realistic,
causing them to shoot away, leaving the part mesh or heavily deforming it, and as such
breaking the model[27].
The contact types determine the method of checking for penetration.
The generally
recommended method is contact keywords containing "AUTOMATIC". This methods
means that both sides of an element is checked for penetration. This makes the contact
more stable, and since the outcome of simulations is often not known in advance, this
allows for significant changes in the contact geometries without causing problems with
nodes not being checked for contact. In shell elements, this method projects the contact
surface from the mid-plane at a distance of half the total thickness, and includes wrapping
around the edges of the shell elements. This is generally referred to as offsetting, and the
specific contact offsetting can be modified outside of the defined thickness. Due to this
method it is essential to have gaps equal to this offset in the initial geometry[28].
If this initial setting is not done, or the geometry does not allow for it in some way,
all initial penetrations are handled in one step. Depending on the settings this is gener-
ally done by first detecting initial penetrations and then moving the slave nodes to the
master node surface, or further depending on the defined thickness or or surface offset.
There are major problems with this, as this method often results in not all penetrating
nodes being handled, and since the movement can significantly deform the slave part[28].
The penetration depth is often artificially limited to prevent large contact forces, and
in order to prevent total penetration in the case of thin geometries or elements such as
shells, and the limit can be changed based on the thickness. Most contacts also allows
for changing the depth of checking, which can be essential for stability in large thickness
geometries and elements[28].

==PAGE:33==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
4
Results and discussion
Since one of the main aims of this project is to facilitate the simulation process, a large
part of the results is the experiences gained through the process. The step by step process
and accompanying explanations and discussions of the steps taken, are as much the results
as the final model and simulated geometry. For this reason the processes, the experiences
gained, and their results, are combined in this section.
For reference, the initial geometries can be found in Section 1. For the purpose of describ-
ing the certain parts of the geometries, certain terms are used. These are here explained,
and illustrated on Figure 13.
Figure 13: Visualization of common terms used to describe parts of the geometry.
Figure 13 uses the step 3 part as an example, but the references are the same for all steps.
After step 4 the part has almost become fully rectangular, but for consistency the terms
short and long radius are used. The rounding on the side, shown in yellow, are referred
to either as the punch edge or 45° degree rounding/edge, in reference to the wall surface
normal, in order to differentiate it from the workpiece edge. The edge of the workpiece
also refer to any remaining flange.
4.1
Measurements and observations
From observing the production samples the following has been determined. According to
the production engineers, the rolling direction is parallel to the long radius. However, on
the initial step, as well as on the flat surface of step 2-4, it is clear that the true rolling
direction is parallel to the shortest radius for the provided samples. This observation is
essential for the modelling of the anisotropy, and is described further in section 4.3.6.
A further important observation is that for step 1 the deep-drawing does not leave a
flange, meaning that the punch travel is sufficient to pull the entirety of the blank through
27 of 125
==PAGE:34==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
the gap between the die and the blankholder. But, for steps 2-4, a significant flange is
present. This is essential for several distinct reasons.
Firstly, the CAD model of the production does not indicate that this should be the case.
By measuring the punch travel as the distance between the punch position in the open
and closed configuration, it is self evident that this should not allow for any remaining
flange. After discussions with the production team, it was eventually discovered that
the punch travel for certain processes is less than the production setup CAD suggests.
That said, even those punch travels do not allow for as much flange as is evident on the
production samples. Due to the geometry of the produced parts, an exact stroke length
cannot be accurately measured, but it can be approximated.
Secondly, the flange thicknesses and geometries have a very significant effect on the the
rest of the geometry. An example of this is when the flange is partially through the gap
between the blankholder and die. Since the edge thickness is much higher in all processes,
this thickening would increase the friction and required punch force, thus stretching the
rest of the part, thus determining likely breakage locations as well as resulting thicknesses
and thickness distributions.
The last important observation is that there is significant springback, deforming the parts
asymmetrically. This is not compensated for in the production, as the low strength of the
material allows for manually deforming the parts to fit the tools, but it is essential for the
simulation. Furthermore, it makes accurate measurements difficult, as it affects the wall
height and edge contours.
The critical dimensions of the final part is the minimum and maximum thicknesses. This
is the objective of the production, however for validating simulation results, the thickness
distribution and profiles would be significantly more useful. According to the company,
the design specifications designate a thickness of 1mm with a tolerance of ±0.1mm. How-
ever, during the run-in, the aim was ±0.5mm. In relation to the simulation validation, the
required measurement accuracy is relative to the deviation. Since much of the simulation
results deviate by more than 0.1mm, 3D scan of the parts were determined to be the
most efficient method of validation. Any more accurate measurements would require sin-
gle point measurements, which would require significantly more time in order to achieve
enough data to accurately compare simulation results and measurements. Furthermore,
many such tools, such as coordinate measuring machines (CMM) do not have the required
spacial mobility.
The most accurate 3D scanner available, which is also capable of scanning relatively large
geometries, is the GOM Atos III. This is an optical scanner capable of taking individual
scans around the geometry and stitching these together to form a pointcloud which can
be stitched together into a 3D scan model. This can then be used in conjunction with the
GOM Inspect software to determine distances between points, and creating visualizations
of the thickness distribution and contours[29].
The accuracy of the measurements are dependent on the magnification, and thus the
lenses. The size of the production samples require the lowest magnification, but the ge-
ometry also requires a very high amount of individual scans, decreasing the probability of
measurement deviations. While it is specified as having a maximum deviation of 0.1mm,
studies of the Atos III scan accuracy using similarly sized shell like objects, indicate a
28 of 125
==PAGE:35==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
much higher degree of accuracy[29]. One study comparing it to a Zeiss Primo 7 CMM
verified according to VDI/VDE 2634 part 3, a form error of 1µm, a diametric error of
6µm, and a length measurement error of 1µm[30]. Another study found that the accuracy
is heavily dependent on the surface. If the surface is smooth, shiny, transparent, or dark
in colour, the measurement deviations could well exceed 0.1mm, but that it could be
negated by spraying the surface with a white anti-reflective matting agent[31].
This coating does add approximately 0.02mm to the thickness results, and since these
samples were a glossy aluminium alloy, it needed to be applied onto the entirety of the
surface. This means that the scanned thicknesses would at least be 0.04mm higher, and
possibly varying depending on the thickness of the coating. Furthermore, for the software
to be able to stitch the images together, stickers had to be applied to the surface. These
are 3mm circular black dots surrounded by a white circle. These have to be spread ran-
domly across the scan surface, with a distribution such that at least three are visible from
all angles. While the software should be able to remove these during post-processing, this
was not the case for many of the dots, but they are very easily noticeable on the scan,
and can thus be disregarded. The coating and stickers can be observed on Figure 8.
The previously described accuracy is significantly higher than is required for most of
the comparisons made here, but due to the large discrepancies, the scan results were val-
idated by comparing with manual micrometer measurements in a set of randomly chosen
points across the parts. The largest observed deviations were 0.06mm, with an average
deviation of approximately 0.04mm, which is consistent with the thickness added to the
scan results by the anti-reflective spray, and were thus determined to be sufficiently accu-
rate for the purposes of comparing the simulation results with the real parts, considering
the magnitude of deviations the simulations show.
As previously mentioned, the post-processing software can create a thickness map over-
layed onto the scan CAD model. The thickness is defined as the distance from a point on
a surface to the closest point within a cone perpendicular to the surface, with a slope of
15° degrees in relation to the perpendicular line. This is done for each of the four parts
resulting from step 1-4. These are shown in Figures (14-17) below.

Figure 14: Scan results of step 1 production sample.
Observing Figure 14a, several spots of increased thickness occur. These are the previ-
ously mentioned reference stickers which the the post-processing program for unknown
reasons could not remove, with the exception of the one large circle seen on 14a, which is
a remnant of the anchoring point of the sample, but which should also be ignored.
It is clear that most of the area shows a small but fairly uniform decrease in thick-
ness, with most of that material being pushed towards the edges causing non-uniform
thickening along the edge. The increase along the edges is not critical in and of itself,
30 of 125
==PAGE:37==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
since the flange will be cut, however, while the the thickness changes in the rest of the
part are small for what could be expected of such a process, a reduction of 0.14mm is
already more than the tolerance allows for in the final part.
The heaviest thickening occurs towards the short and long radius, which is consistent
with normal anisotropy earing behaviour, and the anisotropy constant for this material
being significantly higher at 45° degrees. Since the rounding will become smaller in the
following processes, this is desirable for the first step, as will become evident when ob-
serving the following steps.
Observing Figure 14b, it is clear that the pattern of thickness changes is far from sym-
metric. While not critical, it clearly indicates the occurrence of tool deflection. While
this is to be expected due to step 1 and 2 being in the same press, the asymmetry should
be along one plane. Since step 1 and 2 are side by side, with parallel long radius, one
would expect the press to tilt towards the side requiring least force. This movement can
be seen in the approximate 0.05mm edge thickness difference between the short radius
sides, but the long radius sides differ by approximately the same, wile the four 45° degree
edges do not differ significantly from each other. It can thus be concluded that the press
distinctly tilts around the long and short radius. Furthermore, the tilts have to occur at
separate stages of the process, in order to not show in the corners.

Observing Figure 15 it can be determined that the observed phenomena from in step
1 repeat in step 2. The thinning is however not as extreme, the heaviest thinning is
0.18mm, which is a reduction of 0.04mm between step 1 and 2. Considering that this is
fairly localized due to asymmetry caused by tool deflection, very little thinning occur on
the sides and top between step 1 and 2. The thickening of the edges seem to be more
uniform in relation to the 45° degree corners, not showing a clear distinction between
0°, 45°, and 90°. This is somewhat as expected, as this process is redrawing, meaning
that localized thickening should be evened out.
It should be noted that the small area of extreme localized thinning seen on 15b, is a
32 of 125
==PAGE:39==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
result of a reference sticker, which was improperly handled by the post-processing pro-
gram. It removes the scan-point from the surface where a sticker is detected, and stitches
the surface together based on the surrounding points. If one or more of the sticker points
are not removed, the surface created from these surrounding points will be irregular.
The asymmetry is much more noticeable in this step. This is to be expected as it is
compounded by the asymmetry from step 1, but it does raise the question of why not
negate this effect by rotating the piece 180° between the steps. While the answer to this
is not known, it does indicate that the asymmetry does not cause significant problems for
the production, for them to notice or expend effort correcting. Furthermore, the differ-
ence between sides is approximately the same as in step 1, indicating that it is not the
top or bottom plates themselves deforming, but the relative positioning of the two plates.
According to the production team, they do compensate for this with weights on one side
as well as SHIMS, so it can be surmised that this degree of asymmetry either cannot ac-
curately be compensated for, or that the effects are too small for it to be worth investing
time and money in correcting. This is however puzzling, as this degree of asymmetry
clearly causes changes to exceed the desired tolerances, and since it will inevitably cause
problems in step 3-6, which would require more effort to correct.

==PAGE:40==

(a) Scan thickness map of step 3 showing the

(b) Scan thickness map of step 3 seen from the

(c) Scan thickness map of step 3 showing the full

(d) Colour scale for step 3 scans.
Figure 16: Scan results of step 3 production sample.
Observing Figure 16, the sample has become much more rectangular in shape. As ex-
pected the much sharper corner causes clearly visible changes, showing much heavier
thickening across most of the wall length. While some asymmetry is visible, it is almost
negligible. This is interesting when comparing it with the previous steps, as the finished
part from step 2, which is the initial blank for step 3, clearly showcases a large degree
of asymmetry. There is also no clearly visible signs of ironing on the part. The decrease
in perimeter length across the sides, due to becoming more rectangular, can account for
the slight thickening of the sides, as well as the removal of the localized thinning zones
observed on the step 2 sample, but it indicates that there is almost no thinning caused by
the strain of being pulled through the die-blankholder gap. Since the geometric change
should result in significant force and thus strain, it indicates that other mechanisms coun-
34 of 125
==PAGE:41==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
teract this. There are several mechanisms which could help explain this.
Firstly, as can be seen clearly on the scans, and more clearly on the simulation results
for step 1 and 2, the springback causes the sides to cave in. As described previously, the
springback is not compensated for due to the material being easily formable. After each
step, the part is simply forced onto the die of the next step. This means that throughout
the process, it is likely that the blank contracts around the punch in such a way that the
friction force resulting from it counteracts a majority of the strain on the flat top surface.
This effect could be further enhanced by the residual stress of the material from previous
steps.
Secondly, for much of the process, the minimum gap size is large than the thickness.
That means a very small area of the blank is in contact with the die and blankholder,
and since the degree of deformation decreases for each step, the friction forces would be
small.
A third and somewhat less likely possibility is that these parts could be from different
production runs. While the production specified that these were from the same produc-
tion run, it cannot be ruled out considering the specified rolling direction is perpendicular
to what can clearly be observed by looking at the blank surface grooves. Along this line
of reasoning, it could also be a statistical anomaly considering only one sample of each
step is available for testing.

of both sides

Figure 17: Scan results of step 4 production sample.
Observing Figure 17 it follows the same pattern as step 3. The asymmetry is almost
non-existent here as well, which indicates that the asymmetry from step 2 isn’t being
countered, but that the step 3 and 4 press or tools have a higher stiffness, or that more
effort has been spent on balancing this press.
Since the gap between the punch and
blankholder is 1.2mm for the flat surfaces, and 1.35mm around the punch edges, the
thicknesses indicate that the centering is almost perfect.
Arguably the most interesting observation is that, while the edge thickness varies in
36 of 125
==PAGE:43==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
the same pattern as the step 2 result, the designated gap between die and blankholder
is constant at 1mm according to the production CAD. Considering that the production
clearly specified it as a fixed gap, the scan results clearly contradicts this assertion. It
indicates that either the press for some reason do not function as intended or specified,
or that post-processing of the tools have not been included in the CAD.
4.1.1
Wall length and edge contour
Since the side wall lengths were designed to be longer than needed, such that it could be
cut in the last step due to never being feasible to attain a perfectly uniform wall height
after several deep- and re-drawings, the exact wall heights are not of importance, but
the edge contour is relevant in relation to comparing with simulation results. However,
measuring this for step 2-4 is not feasible, considering their remaining flange geometries,
and the varying punch travel used between simulations. For that reason this is only done
for step 1.
The wall heights are measured across the samples, and the point values are then used
to create a fit for visualizing the contours. This is done for both the physical samples, as
well as for the finished simulation results. These are shown in Figure 18 below.
Figure 18: Graph showing the edge contours of quarter-sections of the first step, as well
as the corresponding contour on the resulting simulation counterpart. The x-axis range
can also approximately be interpreted as being 0° to 90°.
From the contour plot shown on Figure 18, it can be observed that the length of the
wall near the short radii is slightly longer than the physical production part, though
following the same contour shape. This is not the case for the long radius wall length,
being significantly longer in the simulated part. Furthermore, the shape of this section in
the simulated part does not show the same local changes as the production part. Since
the deviation is much stronger on one side, one could assume that this is due to anisotropy.
37 of 125
==PAGE:44==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
During almost the entire time spent on attempting to accurately simulate this step, the
rolling direction was set to be parallel to the long radius based on information obtained
from the production. This however resulted in the same effect as seen on the contour plot,
however being much more significant, showing the simulation long radius wall length being
more than 7mm longer than in the production part. Simulating with the rolling direction
parallel to the short radius results in the contour showed on the plot. The visual differ-
ence in resulting geometry and thickness distribution, as well as the corresponding rolling
direction can be seen on Figure 19.
Figure 19: Visualization of the effects of rolling direction on the simulation results. The
rolling direction is indicated by the black arrows.
While the discrepancy in wall length is not obvious from observing Figure 19, the pattern
of thinning clearly shows the effect of the rolling direction on local wall length differences.
After further investigation of the physical parts this turned out to be the correct rolling
direction. Since changing the rolling direction 90° degrees does not completely negate this
effect, it can be concluded that it is not mainly caused by anisotropy, although it does
most likely have an effect. The results of the tensile tests were recalculated, indicating no
errors, and the simulation was attempted without the effects of anisotropy, showing only
the effect getting stronger. After running many simulations with differing friction, mesh
sizes, and blankholder movement settings, it was concluded that none of these caused the
discrepancy between simulations and physical part. It should be noted that mesh sizes
below ≈0.5mm were not tested, as the simulation and processing times would increase
significantly, and since significant decrease in mesh size did not seem to have any signifi-
cant effect in regards to this effect. This is also the case for the friction, only being tested
in the range of 0.03 −0.15, as anything outside this range would cause total breakage
of the blank or the simulation terminating. This other effects of these parameters are
discussed further in following sections. Since non of this explains the effect, the possible
causes can be narrowed down to tool geometry deviations, differences between the tested
material and production material, or physical material effects not included in the simula-
tion formulation.
In attempting to explain this phenomenon, the most important thing to note is that,
while the contour deviation in step 1 is carried over into the following steps, and while
the exact contours are not measured for following steps, it will become obvious when
seeing the resulting simulation geometries of the following steps, that this phenomenon
38 of 125
==PAGE:45==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
is exclusive to step 1. While this does not necessarily exclude the possibility of the cause
being problems with the simulation settings, it does indicate the most likely causes are,
deviation in tool geometry for step 1, significant residual stresses present in the initial pro-
duction blank, possibly due to incomplete annealing before rolling or heat affected zones
from laser-cutting, some unknown mechanism only occurring in deep drawing and not
redrawing, non-uniform/asymmetrical friction in production, or that the transferring of
deformed blanks in LS-DYNA somehow negates the effect. Most of these cannot feasibly
be tested within the scope of this project. However, based on the observed surface grooves
caused by the rolling, it does seem likely that the friction could vary significantly between
the long and short radii sides. The rolling causes grooves in the surface to appear parallel
to the rolling direction. While sliding perpendicular to these grooves would increase the
friction, worsening wall lengthening effect, by including the effects of lubrication, this
effect is reversed, as groves perpendicular to the sliding direction would significantly in-
crease the occurrence of microplasto hydrodynamic lubrication, significantly lowering the
friction. This could explain some of this discrepancy, as the simulation uses a constant
friction throughout, while the friction at the long radii in reality is most likely much lower
than for the rest of the part. While it can’t feasibly be proven within the scope of this
project, since the average peak distance in the groves is almost perfect for microplasto
hydrodynamic lubrication, since the effect would be the exact opposite on the short ra-
dius, and since this effect is eliminated in later steps where the surface has been altered,
it is assumed that this is the most likely cause. Further investigation into the effects of
anisotropy can be found in section 4.3.6.1.
4.1.2
Tensile tests
The datasheet for the material did not contain much information about the material pa-
rameters outside of composition, processing, density, and Young’s modulus. While a very
simple material model can be constructed based on this, further information was needed
in order to determine the effect of different material models. For that purpose tests were
need, as most of the material models require a strain-stress curve, and furthermore be-
cause most of the anisotropic material models required the 00 deg, 45 deg, and 90° degree
anisotropy constants. Cylinder compression testing was not possible due to only having
1mm plate, and comparing the balanced biaxial stretching and tensile testing, doing ten-
sile testing was the optimal method in relation to experience, available testing material,
and available machinery.
For the testing equipment available, the standard DIN 50125:2009-07 type H was the
most applicable. This standard can be found in Appendix section 8. This standard call
for a 1mm thick plate being cut into 250mm by 30mm pieces with most of the middle
section having a width of 20mm. The most practical options was to have the production
cut these with their in-house laser cutter, and having them shipped to DTU for testing.
While it was specified that it should be from the same plate material used for the pro-
duction, it could not be ascertained where on the plate these samples were cut from, and
the production had previously informed that they had observed significant differences in
material characteristics across the width of the coils.
The setup consists of two fastening points both being two checkered surfaces held closed
by 6 bolts. The machine accurately measures the tensile force for every 0.1s. The speed
is defined as 1mm/s, and is manually stopped after breakage.
39 of 125
==PAGE:46==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
Two extensiometers were used to determine the strain. One to measure the length change,
and one measuring the width change. While the extensiometer measuring the longitudinal
change could be fastened such that it was at the top and bottom most points outside of
the clamps, the extensiometer measuring the width change could only be applied approx-
imately 40mm above the center due to its size.
The machine used was developed by IPU, and is a 15 ton servopress designated IPU1020226,
including a load-cell and displacement tranceducer. The make and model of these exten-
siometers are Epsilon Transverse Extensiomenter model 3575-500M-ST and Epsilon Axial
Extensiometer model 3542-080M-050-ST.
Since the extensiometers were applied manually, the exact positions of each isn’t per-
fect. This is mostly the case for the width change measurement, as the extensiometer
was clamped to the sides of a 1mm thick plate, and any misalignment could cause it to
slip slightly during deformation, possibly casing the width strain to become sightly larger.
Since the fastening points are not restrained in the rotational DOF, and since positioning
and fastening is done manually, this caused some tests to fail as tensile stress was applied.
This was noted, and the results of these samples deviated heavily, and are thus excluded
from the results.
A minimum of 5 tests were performed for each rolling direction, but most have more
tests performed, as either the extensiometers slipped or the clamps had rotated, causing
the resulting stress-strain curves to deviate heavily, despite none of the fractures occur-
ring inside the gauges. Furthermore, after processing the results, many of the stress-strain
curves differed significantly despite being from the same rolling direction samples, requir-
ing more tests to be performed.
Two methods of calculating the strains were applied. This was due the deviations in
the results calling the width change measurements into question.
The first calculation method includes all measurements, calculating the equivalent strain
based on the length, width, and thickness, with the last being derived from volume con-
stancy.
The second method was by calculating the strain solely based on the length
change.
The difference in the resulting curves was essentially negligible, so the first
method is used.
For the following, the the length is defined as the z-axis, the width is defined as the
x-axis, and the thickness as the y-axis, with the extensiometer data labelled as "extz"
and "extx" in millimeters. The strain calculations used are shown below in equations

4.2
Methodology for going from CAD to LS-DYNA model
For going from CAD parts or assemblies to creating a usable and efficient LS-DYNA
model, there are several points to consider.
Since most CAD programs can convert their geomeries to .step or .stl files, importing
them directly into LS-DYNA is possible. LS-DYNA does have the capability to automat-
ically create meshes for the geometry, and does allow for manual tweaking of the mesh.
That said, the process often creates undesirable mesh features, requiring a high degree
of manual reconfiguration and refinement of the mesh. The general observation made
43 of 125
==PAGE:50==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
during the process of meshing these geometries using the build-in tools, is that the more
advanced the geometry is, and the more rounding occur, the amount of effort required
manually refining the mesh increases exponentially.
This method is however superior
when studying the effect of mesh sizes, model testing, and when used on simple geome-
tries. Of the methods attempted during this project, the build-in meshing tools are by
far the fastest and most reliable in these aspects due to the automatic meshing reliably
creating relatively consistent meshes in relation to size, meaning that by defining the de-
sired mesh size and starting automatic meshing, it quickly creates a mesh with very few
mesh elements deviating from the specified size. This however is also the problem. The
automatic meshing only allows for defining a minimum, nominal, and maximum mesh
size. There is no options for preference of structure like quadrilateral mesh elements, and
there is no options for creating finer meshes locally, like for example for non-flat surfaces.
To do this the areas have to be segmented, in order to do separate automatic meshing for
the surface segments. The problems in this are that firstly the imported CAD is consid-
ered as one surface, the process of segmenting the surface areas is very time consuming,
and the connections between two connected surfaces do not automatically align, often
creating free unconnected edges between surfaces. Furthermore, creating segments can in
some cases distort the geometry with no warning of this having occurred.
While the automatic meshing is not ideal for this method, the manual meshing how-
ever is fairly good. The manual mesh editing tools are easy to use and mostly creates
good results. The problems are that during manual mesh configurations it is exceedingly
easy to accidentally create free edges, or undesirable geometries. Since the manual mesh
editing is mostly used to refine coarse meshes, use of the split tools will occur, and many
of the options will result in lines between elements being split.
This however creates
the problem of the surrounding elements, since if for example a line is split, creating a
node on the midpoint of a line, that node is not incorporated into the neighbour element.
Instead, a constraint keyword is added, constraining the new point on the existing line.
For inexplicable reasons this constraint sometimes does not get created, and sometimes
a single constraint can disappear after saving the mode, creating free edges inside a ge-
ometry’s surface. While it is fairly easy to find these by choosing the setting highlighting
free surfaces, in such large meshes as are considered here, often requiring hours of manual
mesh editing, missing such a free edge was not an uncommon occurrence, especially since
