previously finished meshes could break after saving.
The problem with this is that if a simulation is run with such free edges, it will almost
always cause the simulation to terminate, and most of the time this termination occurs
far into the simulation. Returning to see the results of a several day long simulation, only
to see that it has terminated due to errors, and having to fix the mesh and completely
restart the simulation, can become frustrating and very time consuming.
A further problem with manually refining meshes in LS-DYNA is that any new nodes
and elements created will not conform to the original geometry, but rather the initial
meshed geometry. To further explain, if one were to imagine a 90° degree rounding ini-
tially meshed as two radial elements, if the amount of radial element were doubled after
by splitting these, it would still consist of two 45° degree corners. Outside of obtaining
better aspect ratios or less warpage of elements, this makes manually refining non-flat
geometry meshes pointless. It could be done by starting with a fine initial mesh and then
44 of 125
==PAGE:51==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
merging elements, but that can only be done two elements at a time, making it excessively
time consuming.
This lead to the conclusion that, while the LS-DYNA meshing tools could be used for ini-
tial tests of models as well as for refining meshes with few undesirable elements, the best
method would be to import the initial mesh from another program with better meshing
tools. Several programs exist which are dedicated to meshing both free and paid. After
surveying the options it seemed all of these were exceedingly similar in capability. While
it had not yet fully taken place as of this process, the LS-DYNA software is reportedly to
be incorporated into the ANSYS simulation ecosystem. Furthermore, the ANSYS FEM
meshing tools were all included in the educational licences available, and tutorials are
available for specifically using ANSYS meshing in conjunction with LS-DYNA simula-
tion. For that reason this method was tested, and eventually used for all models.
The use of this will be discussed in detail later, but regardless of the meshing tool, the
initial CAD model has to be adapted for meshing. The method is in all likelihood very
similar regardless of the CAD program, but here it will be investigated and described in
relation to the SOLIDWORKS CAD program, since this is both used by the company
and commonly preferred at DTU.
The most efficient approach is taking all parts of the desired process and creating an
assembly. Correctly positioning the parts in relation to each other is easier and more
accurate in CAD programs as compared to doing it later in the meshing program or
LS-DYNA. Furthermore, it facilitates simplification of the geometry, firstly in relation to
utilizing symmetries, secondly in relation to combining several parts which will be sub-
jected to the same settings, and thirdly to obtain clarity as to what parts of the geometry
are necessary, and can thus not be removed in the process of simplifying the geometry.
A good example of this is in this case the top plate, blankholder plate, and blankholder
ring. Even when not considering completely rigid tools, which is not done here, only the
material of the ring would be of importance. All these parts are all completely fixed to
each other in the production, all moving together, and only one surface of each is needed
for the simulation. These three can thus be combined and reduced to a single surface from
the start, significantly reducing the required effort in later steps. For these purposes it is
necessary to convert the assembly to a part, as assemblies do not allow for several of the
tools required. An example of this is if shell elements are to be used. In that case offset
surfaces can be created as desired by selecting surfaces, and the solid parts suppressed
before saving so that only the surfaces are exported. The export should ideally be a step
file or alternatively an stl file. Having all parts in one file can potentially cause problems
later, but for all geometries considered here, the meshing tools can automatically distin-
guish which parts are to be considered separate. The process and effect of simplifications
is discussed further in Section 4.3.3.
The software ANSYS Workbench is used to connect several other tools and organizing
their use by creating a flowchart transferring the finished product to the next software.
An example of the structure used for step 2-4 can be seen on Figure 22.
45 of 125
==PAGE:52==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
Figure 22: ANSYS Workbench interactive flowchart used for step 2-4.
The structure used here is initially creating separate geometry sections. In each of these
the CAD step file is imported and processed. The program used for this is "Design Mod-
eller". It automatically identifies separate geometries, allowing for each to be suppressed
as desired, and it automatically segments the surface based on the CAD and geometry.
These segments can then be manipulated for optimal meshing control. An example of this
is shown on Figure 23a. It should be noted here that merging lines and surfaces can result
in slight geometry distortions. While it happens rarely, small deviations in geometry can
be difficult to detect, and can have unintended consequences. In one such case a slight
deviation caused problems in obtaining proper simulation results. Discovering the cause
of these problems being a slight geometry deviation, as well as fixing it, can be excessively
time consuming, in one such case taking almost 7 days of simulating and testing in order
to discover and fix this. It is therefore recommended to keep alterations of the segments
to a minimum, especially surface merging. It should also be noted that the automatic
segmenting can result in the occurrence of excessively small segments. These are not
visible when observing the part, but these will become obvious during the meshing, as the
surrounding mesh will be extremely fine relative to the rest of the mesh. If such meshing
is observed, the fix is not obvious yet very simple, by simply going back to the segmenting
and zooming in until the small segments are visible, and then merging them with the
surrounding larger surface segments.
After this, the segmented geometry is transferred to meshing in "ANSYS AUTODYN
PrePost". While creating an automatic mesh of uniform size results in slightly inferior
results compared to LS-DYNA’s build in mesher, the strength of this mesher is that the
automatic mesh can be made to conform to several specifications. These include spe-
cific edge sizings and shapes, surface sizings, individual surface mesh preferences such as
quadrilaterals, several settings for refining and punching the automatic mesh, and varying
sizes across a surface conforming to functions of change depending on location. This allows
46 of 125
==PAGE:53==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
for relatively fast creation of meshes conforming well to the geometry without creating
excessive amounts of elements. For the shell elements used in this project, the method
used was setting varied edge sizes such that roundings had significantly finer meshes, and
such that almost the entire surface could be meshed using quadrilaterals. And example
of these divisions and the resulting mesh can be seen on Figures 23b and 23c respectively.
(a) Example of the segmented areas automatically created
for the blankholder of step 4.
(b) Example showing the segmented settings
used for creating an adapted mesh for the step
4 blankholder.
(c) The mesh created by automatic meshing
based on the settings showcased in Figure 23b.
Figure 23: Visual examples of ANSYS meshing showcasing some of the meshing performed
during project.
After creating the mesh it can directly be exported, but for convenience it is here chosen
to use the "Finite Element Modeler". This method is not supported in newer versions,
but it serves as a substitute before the option to directly include LS-DYNA in ANSYS
workbench becomes available.
It is done such that the geometries can be combined,
the meshes automatically recognized as combined surface elements, and exported as an
ABAQUS ".inp" file for easy import in LS-DYNA. Doing it this way causes LS-DYNA to
recognize the assembly as one part, but they can easily and quickly be separated using the
"Move or Copy" tool. Doing this the desired parts, meshes, elements, element types, and
assembly configuration, is ready for defining the keywords for controlling the simulation.
While this method does seem more time consuming, when working with large meshes and
advanced geometries this is the best method found. A further advantage is that making
significant changes to the mesh can be done fairly quickly. If for example a rounding on
one part turns out to not have a sufficiently fine mesh, one can go back, change a couple
of numbers defining the mesh size of that specific section, and simply update and export
the full assembly mesh. This means that even if, using this method, the initial meshing
47 of 125
==PAGE:54==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
is more time consuming, making changes later will require far less effort, especially when
LS-DYNA is eventually Incorporated into the software ecosystem.
4.3
Simulation
The initial step of the simulation was to create models of simpler geometries, in order
to become familiar with the program, the process, and in order to have references for
determining the degree to which the exact geometries and production setup studied here
deviates from similar models.
The first models created were deep-drawing of a completely round cup. This consisted of
three models, one utilizing the symmetry to make a two dimensional model, one full three
dimensional model utilizing shells, and one full three dimensional model utilizing solid
elements. Since all previous experience consisted of working with symmetry model, the
accuracy of the 2D axisymmetrical model had previously shown to be very good. It could
thus be used to validate the results of the 3D models. The solid element model broke
down due to the elements collapsing in on themselves seemingly due to being compressed
between the die and the blankholder. After trying several settings for the movement,
constraints, and contact formulations, the model using shell elements eventually matched
the results of the axisymmetric model, naturally with the exception of the shape of the
flange wrinkles, as the axisymmetric model cannot model this.
This model was then utilized to simulate non-axisymmetric deep-drawing models of vari-
ous geometries. Since the CAD of the production setup did not arrive until several weeks
into the project, an approximate model was created based on pictures taken during a
visit to the production, including doing multi-step simulations. Using these models to
test model settings, everything was made ready for the exact production setup.
While the approach of starting out with simplified geometries is instrumental in learning
how to use the program, and the effects of different settings, it did not prove to be very
useful outside of learning the basics. The reason for this is that the final production ge-
ometry proved itself to be so much challenging, that it wasn’t comparable to the example
geometries, and required an almost completely different model. This was due to multi-
ple factors unexpected factors, such as for example if the blankholder gap is sufficiently
close to the thickness of the blank, then the usable contact models, material models, and
constrains is significantly limited, as well as which specific settings can be used in these.
That means that while the initial models created based on similar geometries need to be
completely reworked. Further amplifying this effect, when the simulations are not based
on real production samples, or other simulations known to be very accurate, if significant
time is spent working on example geometries, there is a natural tendency to adapt the
models based on decreasing processing time. So while it would certainly have been better
to initial construct the model for robustness, the experience gained during this time is
that the appearance of the geometries and setup being similar can be the cause of signif-
icant amounts of effort being wasted on developing optimization, which can only be used
as training. Thus, it would be recommended to only attempt optimization on the true
geometries.
The process of simulating these production steps and the experiences gained during this
48 of 125
==PAGE:55==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
process is what is discussed in the following sections.
4.3.1
Implicit or Explicit in practice
The initial choice of method comes down to what fits the model best. Based on previous
experience and advice from others with significant experience simulating similar geome-
tries, the implicit method was used for the test geometries. When these were functioning
the explicit method was applied in order to determine the general differences in result and
approach.
The main part of this test was that, depending on the model, it can be fairly time-intensive
switching between the two. Several of the settings and keywords only work for one of the
methods. Furthermore, this is often not clear from either the documentation, manual,
or previous experience. One example of this is that the contact types. According to the
documentation, contacts containing "FORMING" is optimized for implicit analysis, but
can be used for explicit analysis. In practice this turned out to be far from true, as using it
for explicit analysis broke any models outside of the absolute simplest models. This is the
case for many other options as well as specific keyword options. While it does sometimes
state in the GUI note that some settings are only for either explicit or implicit analysis,
this is often not the case, and is sometimes only explained in the documentation, or not
at all. This means that, when switching analysis mode in a new model, the time spent
debugging can be extreme. While the effort required is dependent on the initial model,
the reasons for switching often does not occur in the simplest models. As described in Sec-
tions 3.3 and 3.4, the computation time, accuracy, and stability of each method is heavily
dependent on the model setup and geometry. This means that the need to switch often
only occurs far into the simulation process, after having already simulated, optimized, and
altered a model significantly. The factors making it a time-consuming process is, firstly,
that it is often not clear which keyword or setting is the cause of problems, meaning that
the process involves significant trial and error, with the troubleshooting speed dependent
on experience, secondly, the debugging information given by LS-DYNA is severely lacking
in the sense that it often does not give a reason for problems, or does not give an exact
reason instead outputting a general cause, such as shooting nodes detected, and thirdly,
that the problems often do not occur in the start of the simulation. The combination
of these problems compound the effect significantly. An example of this is a simulation
which takes 22 hours, failing somewhere near the end, and not showing why. In that case
the only approach available is to make a list of probable causes and narrow it down. First
by changing as many of those as possible to see if any of those is the culprit, running it
again, and then if any of those were the cause, change half of those back and repeat. Here
it might be the case that there are several problems, which makes the process even more
demanding, or that the attempted fixes themselves introduce problems. If for example
seven iterations of this is required, each taking approximately 20 hours, such a method
switch would take a week. A further aspect making this process more troublesome, is that
if a simulation does not finish or get a proper error termination, it will not move on to the
next queued simulation. In both analysis methods, if the time-step size is adaptive, which
is by far the most optimal setting for both accuracy and speed, problems very frequently
manifests as the simulation getting stuck, in the sense that when it encounters a problem,
it will continually lower the time-step size until it no longer makes practical sense, or hits
a defined lower limit, which is likely to prevent it from finding a solution.
49 of 125
==PAGE:56==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
To make up for this, after much trial and error, the method found to be most effec-
tive, is by having multiple computers on hand, and checking the list of probable causes in
batches distributed between several running simulations. That said, the overall conclu-
sion based on this, is that the optimal approach is to gain sufficient experience in using
LS-DYNA to accurately chose the optimal method in the initial model, and sticking with
that method.
4.3.1.1
Experiences with the implicit method
In the initial sample geometry models, the general experience was that the implicit method
was optimal. Firstly the results of implicit simulations were better, in the sense that they
more accurately aligned themselves with the expected outcomes, and that the time depen-
dent values, such as the force curves, were significantly more stable and realistic. When
looking at the force curves for explicit simulations, peaks are higher, and the force fluctu-
ates constantly, with the magnitude of these fluctuations depending on the mass scaling.
Since the time-step size is dependant on the mass scaling, for similar simulation times
as for implicit, the fluctuations were significant. Furthermore, using the implicit model
allows for using better suited contact models in relation to metal forming.
Due to the experience with the simplified geometries, it was decided to exclusively work
with implicit models for the production geometries. This was however changed later.
With the production geometry the implicit simulations almost always struggled to con-
verge. This manifested as both the time-step size decreasing significantly, and in the
amount of iterations per step becoming relatively large. This lead to a significant period
spent on trial and error troubleshooting, during which may things were determined, and
analysis method related experience are included here.
The default analysis method in LS-DYNA is explicit, so running implicit requires the
keywords *CONTROL_IMPLICIT_GENERAL and *CONTROL_IMPLICIT_AUTO.
In *CONTROL_IMPLICIT_GENERAL the option IMFLAG determines the implicit/ex-
plicit switching, and while it is possible to switch between the two during a simulation,
this has not been attempted. This also allows for setting the initial time-step with DT0.
The experience with changing this is that, when using automatic time-step control, very
little can be gained by changing the initial step from being automatically determined.
The only case where it might be beneficial is by setting it very low if the model tends to
fail in the first couple of steps, and it has already been determined not to be a contact
problem. The rest of the options depend on the model, such as specifying whether its a
springback model, how to handle initial velocities, or which element formulation is being
used.
The keyword *CONTROL_IMPLICIT_AUTO determines the time step control method.
It allows for constant, automatic adjusting, or adjusting following a defined curve with
the IAUTO setting, and max/min limits. It also determines the optimal equilibrium iter-
ation count per time-step with the ITEOPT option. Through extensive experience with
these options, it has been determined that changing most of them from the default values,
should be reserved for the last resort in debugging. In models such as those studied here,
a constant time-step will not work, and if the automatic time-step control determines that
it needs to be excessively large or small, then parts of the model and its settings should be
50 of 125
==PAGE:57==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
changed. The optimal equilibrium iteration count per step ITEOPT and allowable itera-
tion window ITEWIN effectively only changes the magnitude of timestep adjustment and
the limit on the maximum amount of iterations before it retries the step, and that limit
can itself be directly set using a keyword such as *CONTROL_IMPLICIT_SOLUTION.
Except for when these values do not conflict with the iteration limit, changing them
showed no noticeable effect. Setting a minimum time-step is tempting if a model gets
stuck due to very low adapted time-steps, but is only usable in troubleshooting and not
very effective. When the time-step hits the minimum, the amount of iterations just go up.
While not generally found to be useful in this project, it can force the model to reach the
iteration limit instead of excessively lowering the time-step size, which means it results in
a proper error termination. The general experience in this project is that if a maximum
time step is needed, then either something is wrong in the model, or it indicates that one
should make use of one of the many options which are more computationally intensive
and results in increased accuracy. That said, a maximum can be important in dynamic
systems, since limiting the deviations between time-steps increases stability, and it can
be important for the accuracy of the results, since there is no output between steps.
It should be noted that despite what is noted in many manuals and guides, the *CON-
TROL_TIMESTEP keyword had no noticeable effect on the models used here when
running implicit.
While the implicit method gives better results, and can be more stable, if the main prob-
lems of a model is contact related, then implicit models will have significant problems
with convergence. This was exactly the case with this process, considering that almost all
movement results in large sliding surfaces. The problem was that this was also the case
for the example geometries used initially, but resulting in very few problems. For this rea-
son extensive troubleshooting was attempted in order to make the implicit models work.
The constantly reoccurring problem was that it would break down at seemingly random
points in the process. Most commonly manifesting as failure to reach convergence. The
result of this was that while some extreme settings could force the simulations to progress
further, reaching a finished state would only be possible by either using extremely unreal-
istic settings, or accepting extremely unreliable results. A result of this was also that the
simulation time was drastically higher, going from less than 30 minutes for the fairly simi-
lar example geometries, to exceeding 20 hours per simulation for the production geometry.
In the end it was concluded that for models with this degree of complexity in contacts,
including sliding between elements in various states of tension and compression, sliding
over rounded surfaces, and sliding contact on both sides of thickening shell elements, it
simply isn’t practical or feasible to use the implicit method. This is further described in
Section 4.3.4.
4.3.1.2
Experiences with the explicit method
Using the explicit method on the now highly optimized model, it immediately became
evident that this is significantly more suitable for this geometry, or at least for step 1.
The simulation time dropped by a factor of approximately three compared to using the
implicit, and it became possible to progress much further in the process. While it didn’t
immediately result in a fully functioning model, one of the unexpected major advantages
of using the explicit method, is that it moves forward until it fully error terminates in-
51 of 125
==PAGE:58==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
stead of slowing down trying to fix problems, meaning that if a part breaks, or some nodes
shoot away due to contact problems, it can clearly be observed in the resulting graphical
representation. The importance of being able to see exactly what goes wrong cannot be
understated, and significantly increases the troubleshooting speed.
The explicit method is default in LS-DYNA, is and mainly controlled using the *CON-
TROL_TIMESTEP keyword. The main option here is DT2MS which controls the mass
scaling, and thus the time-step size. When the mass scaling is increased, the time-step
size increases but the accuracy decreases.
Determining the correct mass scaling requires a mass scaling study. While this can be
done in several ways, the method chosen here is to compare the y-axis reaction force
curves for one of the rigid parts, usually being the blankholder, and seeing at at which
mass scaling value the curve has converged sufficiently.
Initially mass scaling studies were performed for the example geometries, but through
working with the explicit method, it became evident that the appropriate mass scaling is
highly dependent on the specific model. Since this process is on the limit of what is pos-
sible, most of the mass scaling studies could not show a proper convergence. An example
of this is included below.
Figure 24: Mass scaling study example, performed in the process of attaining a functioning
model for step 1, here testing with no friction.
The first five mass scaling studies showed something along the lines of Figure 24, either
stopping due to the simulation failing, or not showing any real convergence behaviour.
The problem with this is that lowering the mass scaling more than 1e-5 results in unfea-
sible simulation times, here meaning more than 3 days on the available computers, and
mass scaling grater than 7e-5 is too far from converging for it to make sense to use. Ideally
the convergence would determined based on difference from a converged state, but since
52 of 125
==PAGE:59==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
none of them had converged at the finest mass scaling, this is the optimal alternative for
visualisation. It should be noted that sign of the mass scaling only serves to determines
whether LS-DYNA is to automatically adapt the time-step or not.
After a significant amount of time was spent on optimizing the step 1 simulation, the
final mass scaling study was performed, resulting in the curves shown on Figure 25.
Figure 25: Final mass scaling study, performed towards the end of the process of attaining
a functioning model for step 1.
Observing Figure 25 it can be determined that it has almost converged completely at 5e-5,
with almost no noticeable difference between 3e-5 and 1e-5. Based on available guides and
previous experience of multiple people, a mass scaling of 5e-5 is common, and since the
difference in simulation time is fairly significant, this is what was used for most models
during the process, with the final models using 3e-5.
Due to the massive problems with the implicit method, only the explicit method was
used after the initial attempts on step 1, with the exception of the springback model used
after each step.
4.3.2
Solid or shell elements
In the initial example models, both solid elements and shell elements were tested. The use
of each was attempted for the tools and for the workpiece, as well as various combinations
of solid and shell tools and workpiece.
Shell elements are only suitable for thin structures, they require volumes to be converted
to surfaces, and they do not have a stress-strain profile through the thickness, instead
using average stress. Since this processes uses a relatively thin blank, these should not
present any challenges. While solid elements do not have the same geometrical limita-
53 of 125
==PAGE:60==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
tions, and while they can provide stress and strain profiles throughout the thickness, they
are prone to shear locking, and require significantly higher meshing and solution times.
Already for the simple example models, it immediately became clear that solid elements
were not well suited for this purpose. Using solid elements for the tools requires several
times more effort to attain usable meshes. While models would run with automatically
generated meshes, the rounding meshes would not be sufficiently fine to avoid geometric
locking. The time required to refine the mesh is too large to warrant this approach. Fur-
thermore, the benefits of using solid element tools, is only applicable when using non-rigid
tools. When the tools are rigid, only the initial geometry of the working surfaces has an
effect on the results. While nodes and elements of a rigid part do not add significant
computing time, the immense amount of additional nodes need to use solid elements do
increase the simulation time. Since the amount of added nodes is dependent on the model,
and since there are many other factors affecting the simulation time, an exact relation-
ship has not been determined, but comparing several solid and shell models, the increase
experienced in this project is in the range of 2% −19%, with the majority being below
5%. This relationship only applies to using solid elements for the rigid tools, not the blank.
Considering that the only tangible benefit to using solid elements for the tools, in this
case, would be the ability to model tool deflection, and the very significant effort required
to create these models, the effect of this was only tested in simpler example models. Using
non-rigid tools prevents the use of several keywords which can control tool movements.
The only way of moving the non-rigid tools, is to designate movement of node or segment
sets, or applying load. Applying load does not ensure constant movement, and defining
movement of node sets is very likely to cause to model to break, thus requiring significant
troubleshooting. Applying constraints has the same problem, since constraining a full part
in any DOF defeats the purpose of using non-rigid tools. Considering the tool geometries,
the materials of the tools and workpiece, the extreme increase in simulation time, and the
forces involved, it was determined that making such a model work as intended did not
justify the required time. Due to the large differences in material strength, and the size
of the tools, except for the top and bottom plate of the press and topography changes,
the production setup is essentially using rigid tools, so the loss of accuracy should not be
significant. Full 3d solid element tools would also be needed for simulating with temper-
ature, but this is not relevant for the process in question.
Using solid elements for the blank was attempted. This was determined not to be possible
for this process, and was only attempted on step 1. Compared to shell elements, since
the total amount of nodes is doubled, the simulation time is significantly increased as
well. After that, for each element added in the thickness, the node total is increased by
the same amount of nodes as a shell element model would have required, also having the
same corresponding effect on the required simulation time. Since the amount of elements
need to avoid geometric locking for shell elements is already very demanding, the increase
was determined not to be feasible. The effect of geometric locking was also found to be
higher for solid elements, so the mesh size has to be smaller than for using shell elements.
Another challenge of using solid elements for the workpiece is that the mesh size has an
upper limit approximately equal to the material thickness. If this is not followed, the
aspect ratio of the elements will not be sufficient for them to function. With all of this
taken into account, the simulation time increases beyond what was considered feasible.
54 of 125
==PAGE:61==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
Despite that, running such models were attempted. One of the main problems is that
8-node elements do not have a sufficient resistance to shear stress and compression. As
soon as the blank enters the gap between blankholder and die, the impressive stress and
shear stress caused by being pulled through a closed gap cases the solid elements to col-
lapse in on themselves, breaking the model. This can somewhat be remedied by increasing
the hourglassing resistance and stiffness, but they need to be increased far beyond what
is accurate or reasonable. It also requires the viscous dampening to be extremely high,
and the penalty formulation to be unrealistically soft, as any occurrence of local nodal
force peaks causes the elements to collapse. This could theoretically also be remedied by
increasing the amount of elements in the thickness, but having any more than a single
element thickness would not be feasible here in regards to simulation time, especially
considering that increasing the amount to two most likely wouldn’t be sufficient, as the
general rule is that for bending behaviour, there should be at least 4-5 elements through
the thickness. An example of the solid element collapse can be found on Figure 26.
(a) Initial state for reference. Left is with blankholder included, and right is without.
(b) Last stage before error termination, showcasing the cause of termination.
Figure 26: Example of the cause of error termination in almost all attempts at using solid
elements for the workpiece.
Due to the previously mentioned difficulties with using solid elements, shell elements are
exclusively used beyond initial attempts of step 1 modelling. For this type of process,
especially with something like a flange being pulled through a gap, if the available com-
puting power is not beyond a reasonably high-end personal computer, or if the user does
not have extensive experience with using solid elements, using solid elements is not rec-
ommended. This is especially the case if there is no significant and specific reason for why
the results obtained using shell elements does not result in the desired accuracy.
55 of 125
==PAGE:62==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
4.3.3
Effects of simplification of geometry
Simplifying the model as much as possible, without sacrificing the accuracy of the essen-
tial desired results, is an essential part of simulating. If the process possesses symmetric
properties it is essential to make use of it. Optimally this symmetry should include all
aspect of the model, but slight differences can be omitted if their effects are not significant
in relation to the desired results.
The most effective geometric simplification is utilizing axisymmetric features, such as
for example deep-drawing of a completely round cup, which can be reduced to a two-
dimensional simulation. This is what was initially done when working with the simplified
geometries. Comparing it to a full 3d-simulation, the simulation time is several magni-
tudes lower. The specific time reduction is dependent on the geometry and settings used,
as the options available to asymmetric models are different, but with the large cups tested
in this projects, the reduction ranged between 80% and 90%. It is known from previous
experience testing this, that axisymmetic models of deep-drawn cups is highly accurate in
relation to wall height, wall thickness, thickness distribution, and forces. Looking at the
results, these differed fairly significantly in the three-dimensional model. The axisymmet-
ric model, despite starting as an axisymmetric geometry, could necessarily not model the
asymmetric effects such as folding of the flange. If the effects of anisotropy are low, and
if the geometry allows for it, reducing to a 2D axisymmetric model is the most desirable
option.
For most models the highest possible degree of geometric simplification is the quarter
or half model. Metal forming of highly asymmetric geometries is in itself not desirable,
as the effects become significantly more difficult to predict, and this is compounded if
simulation work is desired. It was decided in the initial steps of simulating the production
geometry, that a quarter model would be the base of all further studies. When using a
quarter geometry, the simulation time is effective also reduced by three quarters. The time
required to set up the model is also significantly reduced. Properly utilizing symmetry
planes does required slightly more work to set up in regards to the model, and process-
ing of the initial CAD. This is however more than offset by the time saved in refining
the mesh. Since much of the time required is in refining and optimizing the model, the
symmetry plane options can be reused, so changing the mesh or the geometry becomes
the main factor in time required. Using a quarter model has the additional benefit of
decreasing post-processing time, as the section plane view option is not well implemented
in LS-DYNA. The main detriment is that non-symmetric effects cannot feasibly be mod-
elled. In this process, that would for example constitute the top and bottom plate tool
deflection, as described in Section 4.1. Another detriment is that the results near the
symmetry planes seem to deviate due to non-symmetric effects. Theoretically the effects
of this should be negligible, but it is clear that it has a significant effect here. The main
effect of this can be observed on Figure 27.
56 of 125
==PAGE:63==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
(a) Picture of the step 1 edge geometry near the symmetry plane indicated with the red line.
(b) Edge geometry resulting from the in a quarter model simulation of step 1, corresponding to
the location on the production sample shown on Figure 27a.
Figure 27: Edge geometry deviation between production sample 27a, and the correspond-
ing simulation 27b.
Despite extensive attempts at correcting this deviation, no settings indicate an effect on
remedying the deviation seen on Figure 27. While this might be a cause of the the previ-
ously mentioned discrepancy between local friction, it seems most likely that its a cause
of all the nodal constraints required in the symmetry plain.
The settings required for optimal utilization of symmetry planes are as follows. All parts
which are in contact with the planes need to be restrained from moving out of them. For
the tools, if they are rigid, they need to be constrained to movement in one axis, as in all
DOF except one allowing for displacement. The most efficient way of doing this is using
the settings CON1 and CON2 in the material card 020-RIGID. CON2 defined rotational
constraints, and needs to be set to 7 for fully constrained. CON1 defines displacement
constraints. Depending on the required movements, creating two materials are usually
needed, one for fixed material with CON1 = 7, and one for moving tools, like the punch
57 of 125
==PAGE:64==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
and blankholder here, with CON1 = 6. For the blank/workpiece, or if the tools are not
rigid, the nodes on the plane need to be constrained. The most efficient approach is first
creating the required node sets with the entity creation tool, and then using the keyword
*BOUNDARY_SPC_SET for each set of nodes, defining the constraints. This is only
possible for a defined coordinate system. Using the global coordinate system is the most
efficient, keeping this in mind from the CAD assembly to the simulation. It is possible
to define the constraint in relation to locally defined coordinate systems, but the general
experience doing this is that this approach is prone to human error. The nodes need to
be constrained in all but one rotational and one displacement DOF. For a ZY-plane, this
would be all DOF exept DOFY, DOFZ, and DOFRX. Problems can occur at the inter-
section, as some nodes can be part of both sets, possibly casing singularities, but this has
not been the case for this process, except for if very significant deformation occur near
these nodes. Problems can also occur if nodes are included in the set, which are not in
the symmetry plane. The finer the mesh, the more care is needed in selecting the nodes
for the node sets.
Most contact options have settings for this setup, like the ISYM option in the B card
of *CONTACT_AUTOMATIC_SURFACE_TO_SURFACE, which exclude nodes and
faces with boundary constraints from the contact formulation. The use of this setting is
generally not needed, and in some cases increase chance of singularities occurring.
With these constraint, assuming that all other movements are not out of the symmetry-
planes, the model will run as intended. An illustration of the setup can be seen on Figure
28.
Figure 28: Symmetry setup of step 1, which is also used for all subsequent steps. The red
and blue lines of nodes visualize the YZ-plane and XY-plane node-sets respectively.
If possible, utilizing a quarter model is highly recommended.
58 of 125
==PAGE:65==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
4.3.4
Effect of contact formulations and settings
The contact formulation used, and its settings, are one of the main issues in these pro-
cesses. For almost all steps, the contact options had to be altered in some way to either
prevent the blank from breaking, or to prevent to model from error terminating.
When choosing the most fitting contact type for these types of processes, the main aim
should be to increase stability, with a minimum of desired accuracy. There are a multi-
tude of contact models available specifically for metal forming, but as many of these are
limited to very specific operations, only general contact options have been attempted here.
As multiple surfaces are in contact for the blank, and since the exact deformation patterns
were not know at the beginning, *AUTOMATIC_SURFACE_TO_SURFACE, and its
variants are tested here. The "AUTOMATIC" setting is stable, and the recommended
choice for surfaces using shell thickness offsets, as they are able to project the con-
tact surface to account for this. Furthermore, if segment-based projection is required,
which it is here, the choices are limited to *AUTOMATIC_SINGLE_SURFACE, *AU-
TOMATIC_SURFACE_TO_SURFACE, and various FORMING contacts. These also
include the default option to model shell edges as beams, and sufficient extension of sur-
faces to cover shell corners.
There are three relevant additional forms of *AUTOMATIC_SURFACE_TO_SURFACE.
These are ..._SMOOTH, ..._MORTAR, and FORMING_.... The smooth contact can cre-
ate curve fitted surfaces to more accurately represent curved surfaces. While this would be
useful here, attempting to use it for any of the attempted models result in error termina-
tion. This is was later discovered to be because the SMOOTH setting can only be used in
FORMING contacts wherein the master side is rigid and in the case of SMP. The mortar
contact type is mostly relevant for studying surface wear and conditions, so it is not used
here. The "forming" contact types are created for metal forming, for improving perfor-
mance in metal forming. As metal forming applications often represent difficulties for the
contact formulation, the "forming" setting allows for softer contacts and different han-
dling of penetration. An example of this is that it allows for a penalty formulation which
scales the reaction forces exponentially in relation to penetration instead of linearly. This
allows for slight penetration, which is often needed in metal forming simulation, without
locking the elements, and without the contact formulation breaking down.
The contact type *CONTACT_FORMING_SURFACE_TO_SURFACE has been found
to be optimal for implicit models, as the softer contact formulations are almost a strict
requirement for reaching convergence. These settings are however only available for im-
plicit simulations. According to the manual, they should also be possible to use in explicit
models, despite them not being created with this purpose in mind, but for any of the mod-
els attempted here, using these in explicit models results in error termination as soon as
contact is made.
Since all models, except for initial attempt in step 1, use the explicit method, *AU-
TOMATIC_SURFACE_TO_SURFACE is used for all final models, and is the focus
here.
The only strictly required required cards for this contact is cards 4, 5, and 6.
59 of 125
==PAGE:66==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
Card 4 requires defining the slave and master. Here, the tool should always be mas-
ter, and the workpiece the slave. The most efficient way of implementing this is with
SSTYP/MSTYP = 3 for using part ids, and then defining the relevant part ids in SSID
and MSID. It is here recommended to set MPR = 1, which defines that the reaction
forces of the master should be saved in the output. This is essential for understanding
what happens during the process, and troubleshooting contact instabilities[32].
Card 5 defines the friction constants.
The first option FS defines the static friction
coefficient. This should not be misinterpreted as only acting as theoretical static friction,
as in only at the beginning of sliding action. The best approach found here is to use FS
as the only friction coefficient, as defining FD and DC are very prone to causing contact
problems for these models. Rhe defined friction coefficient depends on the model, but is
in the range of FS = [0.05, 0.1] in the final models. Defining the Coefficient for viscous
friction VC is not strictly required, but is very useful in preventing element locking, as
it effectively limits the maximum friction force. Physically, this would be the friction
becoming so high that the the material prefers to slide on itself, starting to shear. The
limiting force is calculated as Flim = VC · Acont, with Acont being the segment area of
segments contacted by the nodes in contact. The yield stress in shear is calculated as
VC = σ0
√
3, with σ0 being the yield stress of the slave. For the given material this results
in ˜80MPa
√
3
= 46.2MPa →VC = 46.2, with σ0 = σ0.2 ≈80MPa based on the averaged
stress-strain curve. The Viscous damping coefficient is further explained in section 4.3.4.1.
The last relevant setting is PENCHK, which is the small penetration in contact search
option. This is not of high importance here, but setting it to PENCHK = 1 slightly im-
proves contact stability. This setting means it checks for contacts exceeding the segment
thickness times the maximum penetration distance XPENE[32].
The last mandatory card, card 6, defines the various scaling factors. The only relevant
options to change from their default values are the scaling factors on penalty stiffness
SFS and SFM. If these are too low, penetrations will become too deep, causing contact
problems, and if these are too high, the reaction forces will be too high, causing contact
problems. Generally, if the tool mesh sizes are constant, a finer blank mesh will require
the values to be lower, and a coarser blank mesh will require them to be larger. No direct
correlation has been found, but for the models attempted here, these are in a range of
SFM/SFM = [0.5..3]. The general experience here is that the best approach is starting
with a value of SFM/SFM = 2, and increasing or decreasing from there based on how
fine the blank mesh is. More slave nodes in contact generally mean that penalty reaction
forces will be higher, as it’s magnitude is relative to the number of nodes, and thus more
likely to cause problems when penetration occur. Meaning, that lowering the penalty
scaling is necessary for when the amount of nodes in contact increases.
Cards 7-14 are optional and can be activated by ticking the relevant boxes.
Few of
these options are relevant for these models, but the important ones are discussed in the
following sections.
4.3.4.1
Viscous damping coefficient
In dynamic systems free vibration will occur, and viscous damping is needed as an expo-
nential decay of the oscillation. in LS-DYNA this also helps suppress hourglassing modes.
60 of 125
==PAGE:67==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
For sheet forming simulation this is needed to avoid undesirable oscillation in contacts.
The contact damping is applied perpendicular to the contacting surfaces. VDC is an
integer defining the viscous damping coefficient in percent of critical oscillation. For most
LS-DYNA simulations a value of VDC = 10 is suitable. According to the manual and
LS-DYNA support guides, viscous dampening of VDC = 60-70 is recommended. Through
experimenting with this during the process, using VDC ≥40 is not desirable, as it starts
affecting more than the contact stability. An example of this is that if the punch-blank
contact has a viscous dampening above 60, the effective punch movement will stop being
linear, as the contact oscillates between allowing very high penetration, and very little,
causing the blank to accelerate and decelerate significantly throughout the process. While
this is very beneficial for overcoming element locking, especially in implicit models, the
process becomes difficult to control, and can have unforeseen consequences. The experi-
ence here is that the ideal value is VDC = 10, and if more than VDC ≥40 is needed,
other methods of troubleshooting should be attempted[32][33].
4.3.4.2
Soft constraint options (SOFT)
The soft constraint option, SOFT, defines the penalty formulation. For most applications,
the default penalty formulation is the optimal setting. However, if the meshes cannot be
refined further, either due to geometrical constraints or computing constraints, a softer
formulation might be required. Using a softer formulation lowers the chance of element
locking, at the cost of allowing more penetration, and thus deviating from the true geome-
tries. Here, this is relevant with very coarse meshes for either the tool or blank in the area
of sliding over a curved surface. Here setting SOFT = 2 allows for several segment-based
contact options, which can lower the chance of contact instabilities. Using this setting
with SBOPT = 2 or SBOPT = 5, can lower the chance of contact instability at the cost
of accuracy and simulation time. Using SOFT = 4 defines the interface stiffness as based
on the nodal mass and the global time step size, and changes the relationship between
penetration and reaction forces. It is needed for "forming" contacts, in order to activate
the forming option. Comparable accuracy can be achieved for SOFT = 4, if the penalty
for penetration is significantly increased, but the increase in stability is small, and only
applies to implicit formulations[32].
4.3.4.3
Additional settings of importance
The MAXPAR setting in card 9 can be important for very large elements, or large angles
between elements. This defines the parametric coordinate in segment search, meaning how
elements are extended to account for corner bending. Larger values increase stability, as
it reduces the chance of elements locking, but at the cost of simulation time. Generally
changing this has only been beneficial if large angles between elements are causing contact
problems[32].
The DEPTH setting in card 9 can be increased to 2 for improved accuracy as the cost of
higher simulation time. While the accuracy aspect has not been experienced, setting this
to 2 can slightly improve contact stability in some cases[32].
The SNLOG setting in card 10 defines whether shooting node logic is enabled. When
enabled, the first cycle that a slave node penetrates a master segment, that node is
moved back to the master surface without applying any contact force. Disabling this
by SNLOG = 1, is recommended for all models similar tho the ones attempted in this
61 of 125
==PAGE:68==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
project, as this is likely to cause contact problems[32].
If a MORTAR contact is used, the IGAP setting is enabled, allowing for different pro-
gressive stiffness scalings for large penetrations. If large penetrations cannot be avoided
in an implicit model, this can be very useful in improving convergence[32].
4.3.4.4
Bucket sorts (BSORT)
The BSORT setting defines the number of cycles between bucket sorts. In practice this
means how often the contact is checked. LS-DYNA manuals and guides recommend val-
ues of 10 −15 for the specified contact type used for metal forming. This is however very
far from the experience during this process. Using BSORT = 0 for automatically deter-
mining this interval, result in too high values for these models. A setting of BSORT ≤5
is needed for most models and contacts, in order to prevent contact issues, and for later
steps, a setting of BSORT ≤2 is required. Using BSORT = 5 does not significantly affect
the simulation time in relation to BSORT = 0, but using lower values exponentially affect
the simulation time[32].
Since LS-DYNA was initially developed for impact analysis, the time-steps required for
this purpose are very small, meaning that higher BSORT values are applicable, as less
happens between steps. However, for these relatively long simulations, in relation to pro-
cess time-frame, too much happens between steps for any significant skipping of contact
checking between steps. This is indicates why letting LS-DYNA automatically define this
interval results in underestimating the required frequency[32].
As using values below BSORT ≤5 significantly increases the simulation time, it might
be beneficial to define this using a curve, such that it only lowers for the steps in which is
faces challenges, but this requires knowing when and where exactly problems occur, and
has not been attempted in this process.
The overall experience here is that if a model is encountering contact problems, which are
not clearly related to element locking, the first attempted solution should be to change
BSORT = 0 →5, and then BSORT = 5 →2, before attempting other changes.
4.3.5
Effect of mesh size and shape
The main issues when determining which mesh size and shape to use is time, accuracy,
and feasibility. Generally, a finer mesh improves the accuracy of the result and decreases
the risk of instability, but at the cost of increasing the simulation time. Adversely, having
a coarser mesh will decrease simulation time, at the cost of stability and accuracy. The
focus of this section is to ascertain the balance where the model is stable and sufficiently
accurate while minimizing the required simulation time.
4.3.5.1
Element locking
Focusing first on the feasibility, this mainly concerns how to avoid element locking. This
refers to when the calculated nodal displacements are much lower than they should be,
most commonly caused by too high shear or pressure. It is most common in lower order
elements since they simply are not capable of representing the displacement correctly.
This can be divided into shear locking, pressure locking, and membrane locking. Shear
62 of 125
==PAGE:69==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
locking is usually caused by bending, pressure locking occurs when the elements are com-
pressed in directions in which they are incompressible, and membrane locking is caused
by bending deformation accompanied by mid-plane stretching, and occur when the mem-
brane energy is too high compared to the bending motion to allow it to deform. For the
geometries here, all these have been experienced.
In LS-DYNA, these all present in a similar manner, with only the location and sur-
rounding geometry indicating which is happening. In implicit models this presents as the
inability to find a converged state, as it continually tries to iterate or lower the time-step
to find solutions to problems it cannot solve, and for explicit models, this presents as
elements getting stuck, causing surrounding elements to deform unreasonably.
Whether or not element locking is occurring can be difficult to determine, as the lock-
ing is often not complete, as in no displacement occurring, but rather shows as several
elements experiencing difficulties in trying to displace properly, causing resulting nodal
forces to become unreasonably high. Whether an element breaking is due to the stresses
of the process exceeding the strength of the material, or if its due to local elements locking
and causing force spikes, and thus stress spikes exceeding the material strength, can only
truly be determined by tracking the individual nodal forces. Looking for the problematic
elements by tracking the forces of the individual nodes is not a practical approach, as it
requires finding the problematic element, and then somehow determining that the forces
or energies are unreasonable in relation to the process. Determining that locking is occur-
ring can usually be done qualitatively by visually assessing which elements are comprised
of nodes which are too heavily constrained. Obvious examples could be a triangle or
warped element trying to bend over a sharp edge, or a 1.4mm thick element trying to
enter a 1.2mm gap.
The mechanisms differ between triangular and quadrilateral elements, with triangular
elements being more likely to cause locking problems. The main reason for this is that
triangular elements have less nodal degrees of freedom compared to quadrilaterals. When
using automatic meshing, it is difficult to avoid triangular elements occurring, and man-
ually removing them results in warped quadrilaterals, which is not much better in this
aspect.
During this simulation process, several measures have been found which affect the chance
of locking occurring. Naturally the main aspect is the element size of the blank/workpiece.
Changing this to avoid locking is however not very useful, as it increases the simulation
time significantly, and since the natural course would be to have as fine a mesh as the
given time-constraints allow for. As this is approach is limited, the other is changing the
tool mesh size. When using rigid tools, making the tool mesh finer does not significantly
alter the simulation time. This solution does however come with some problems. The
workpiece and tool meshes should ideally be of similar size in order to avoid contact prob-
lems. This is mainly related to possible instability caused by trying to calculate reaction
forces between a surface with many nodes and one with few. Another problem is that
the effective radius of the rounding is slightly decreased, as the surfaces and edges of the
larger workpiece elements will need to be able to penetrate the tool mesh. However none
of these problems have shown themselves to be significant for any of the models used
here. The effect of the tool element sizes around the edges were studied for step 1. For
63 of 125
==PAGE:70==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
any meshes small enough to avoid locking, no significant deviations could be detected. If
the angle change between elements is more than 10° degrees, the punch force, and thus
draw-in, force will increase, but if such a coarse mesh is used for the roundings, locking
is very likely. The consensus between all the models is that for a 90° turn, approximately
20 divisions, or approximately 4.28° degrees per edge across the section, will ensure a
good result. Since lowering the the tool mesh size seemed to have very little effect outside
of a maximum size for stability, in the final models of all steps, the tool mesh near the
roundings is significantly finer than the workpiece.
The element size on flat tool surfaces had no effect, except for when significant wrin-
kling occurred, combined with a very soft contact model, and very large size difference
between tool and workpiece.
One of the observations made while studying the effect of the blank mesh size sliding
over a tool rounding, is that an approximate ratio limit exists between the tool radius
and mesh size.
It was determined, that a ratio between workpiece element size and tool rounding radius
exceeding mesh-sizewp/rtool > 0.526 will cause instability and prevent implicit conver-
gence. While a ratio of between 0.526 and 0.263 is likely to cause instability for implicit
convergence. The specific limit depends heavily on the mesh shape, as occurrences of
warped or triangular elements increases the chance of instability. This discovery is based
on the stepped rounding of the punch, as it bends the blank first with a rounding of
10.2mm followed by a rounding of 15.2mm. While attempting a convergence study, it
was found that different meshes and mesh sizes failed at different points of the process.
Any mesh size above 8mm would fail almost immediately. For very large mesh sizes,
> 8mm, it fails at the first rounding, and for large mesh sizes, ≈5mm, it would fail at
the second step. A mesh size of 4mm could finish without error termination, but often
showing errors in the contact formulation. That said, the 4mm mesh would also result in
error terminations later due to significant warpage of elements, or due to larger triangular
elements trying to move over the roundings. It was also found that for implicit models,
having the tool rounding mesh be larger than the workpiece mesh almost guaranteed the
occurrence of instability, but having the rounding mesh be finer had no effect on stability.
Here it should be noted that if the tool mesh on roundings is finer than the blank mesh,
the contact formulation used has to allow for significant penetration of edges and faces,
or only detect slave node penetration. Generally however, being forced to use such coarse
meshes for the workpiece is indicative of other problems with the model, and if locking is
very clearly causing problems, like the workpiece tearing apart, then the mesh will need
to be significantly finer to completely avoid the effects.
64 of 125
==PAGE:71==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
Figure 29: Example of the optimal found tool meshing geometry, showing the step 3
blankholder.
An example of the optimal tool mesh size and shape for workpiece meshes of 2mm 1mm
and 0.5mm can be observed on Figure 29. Creating tool meshes using quadrilaterals with
sides parallel to the arcs of the 3D curves, and parallel to the radial directions, creates the
best approximation of the true shape. Ensuring that the quadrilateral sides are parallel
and perpendicular to the movement direction of the sliding part decreases the chance of
contact problems. Furthermore, the tool meshes on both sides of a single sliding element,
like the meshes of the blankholder and die, or blankholder and punch, should be as similar
as possible in shape, in order to prevent contact problems, or very local spots where the
gap size varies due to offset nodes or elements. Sticking to a pattern such as the one
described here, and shown on Figure 29, when meshing the tools, significantly lower the
chance of encountering such problems.
4.3.5.2
Accuracy and time
One of the main ways of determining the optimal mesh size is by performing convergence
studies. The method used was by comparing the force curves of the blankholder or punch
of the models using different mesh sizes. Since the mesh size is carried over from step 1,
all convergence studies on mesh size is performed on the step 1 model.
The main problem here is that simulating the step 1 model will result in error termi-
nations or the blank breaking for all but a few very specific keywords and settings. Due
to this, every time a new alteration was found which could cause the process to go fur-
ther without breaking, a convergence study was performed. The reason for this, is that
fundamental changes to the model, like changing the contact model or its settings, the
convergence study results would change significantly. Furthermore, very few of the con-
vergence studies showcased significant convergence. The natural conclusion based on this
is that the meshes were simply too coarse. This should however be considered relative to
the simulation times. Going below a 1mm mesh, as understood in the average element
edge being 1mm, the simulation time was somewhere between 5 and 8 days. Such times
65 of 125
==PAGE:72==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
were not considered feasible.
The best meshsize convergence study was the second last study performed on step 1
using the final model settings as described in the end of Section 4.3.8.1. The results of
this can be seen on Figure 30.
Figure 30: Punch force as a function of time for 2mm to 6mm mesh sizes using the explicit
method.
Observing Figure 30 it can be seen that the 2mm and 3mm meshes have more or less
converged until some form instability occurs for the 3mm mesh at t = 41.5s, causing
them to start deviating. This would indicate that the point of convergence is slightly
finer than a 2mm mesh. However, the reason a 1mm and a 0.5mm mesh is not included,
is because the previous convergence studies showed that any mesh finer than 2mm breaks
either immediately after punch contact is made, or very early in the process. It was later
determined, that this was due to the contact requiring a lower BSORT value, as more
nodes being present in the problematic contacts amplify their effects. This is further
explained in Section 4.3.4.4.
Figure 30 also shows a general trend in the convergence studies, of coarser meshes ter-
minating earlier. As the 4mm mesh curve shows however, this trend is not constant, as
some coarser meshes do manage to finish without error termination. In convergence study
run before this, the only mesh size which managed to reach a normal termination was the
4mm mesh running explicit, with 1mm, 2mm, and 3mm meshes failing for both implicit
and explicit, and the 4mm mesh running implicit also failing. The only mesh sizes which
managed to finish without errors were 4mm, 2mm, and later 0.5mm meshes. Of these,
the 4mm mesh was too coarse to function in the step 2 model.
Since no definite quantitative amount is found, a qualitative conclusion must be made.
For a similar process with similar dimensions, a mesh size between 1mm and 2mm will
be on the edge of convergence, and a mesh of 0.5mm to 1mm will be on the edge of fea-
sibility time-wise if no greater computing power is available, or if it is for a model meant
66 of 125
==PAGE:73==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
for education, troubleshooting, or evaluating model settings.
4.3.5.3
Adaptive meshing
Adaptive meshing has been attempted at several stages of this simulation process, to
limited success.
In LS-DYNA, adaptive meshing can be utilized by defining the keyword *CONTROL_AD-
APTIVE, and setting ADPOPT = 1 for the relevant parts in *PART_PART.
All options for the adaptive meshing, the frequency, tolerances, sizes, times, etc. are
defined in *CONTROL_ADAPTIVE. For these models, the only somewhat required set-
ting here is ADPOPT = 2, as the default form of defining which elements are to be refined
does not seem to work as intended in this case.
Using adaptive meshing can be desirable here, in order to prevent element locking, as
it can refine warped elements before they lock, or if timed correctly, refine elements as
they warp during locking. As this is a prominent failure mechanism for these processes,
this might be a desirable setting.
There are however multiple problems with using adaptive meshing for these models. The
difference in warping angles between elements that will cause locking is very slight, mean-
ing that setting a feasible tolerance is difficult. If too many elements are refined, the
overall simulation time will be heavily affected, and if the adapt size is not at least four
times smaller than the overall element size of the part, it won’t have an effect. Further-
more, even when using adaptive meshing with relatively very small tolerances, the effect
on preventing locking has proven itself to be almost negligible. Adaptive meshing also
does not work well with multi-step processes, as the adapted elements are prone to prob-
lems when exported to a new model. Firstly, the new created nodes are constrained to
point on surrounding lines. These constraints sometimes are not defined properly when
the workpiece is exported, meaning that each finished workpiece has to be thoroughly in-
spected for free edges. Any occurrence of free edges on the surface will cause subsequent
simulations to fail. Secondly, while in a single model the increased simulation time can
be limited by adapting far into the process, the added elements will increase the following
process’ simulation times significantly. Thirdly, as the adapted elements break the fairly
uniform mesh pattern of the blank, they tend to cause stress or thickness concentrations.
And lastly, slight problems can occur when an element is refined, as the the thicknesses
sometimes are not distributed properly, causing small overly thick elements.
For these reasons, the recommendation in relation to these types of models, is that using
adaptive meshing should be limited to testing for if element locking is causing a given
problem, so that other measures can be taken to remedy this for the final model. The
most useful measure found here is to simply go back and refine the full initial blank mesh.
If such problems are found in later steps, as the bends become sharper and elements have
been warped by previous steps, it is tempting to just automatically refine the blank at
the start of the simulation, but the experience here is that it is more useful to start over
from the initial step with a finer blank mesh.
67 of 125
==PAGE:74==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
4.3.6
Effect of material models and their settings
For the tools, the applied material is 020-RIGID or *MAT_RIGID. This model is required
for using rigid tools. It requires the density, Young’s modulus, and Poisson’s ratio, of the
tool material, as well as the constraints. Attempting to use different material constants
has not shown itself to have any noticeable effect. Despite this, for these simulations,
the following have been defined RO = 0.00773, E = 2.050e+05, and PR = 0.3, as de-
fined by the tool material datasheet[34], and the consistent units system used (here being
ton, mm, s, N, MPa, etc.).
The constraints are defined by setting CMO = 1, and
then CON1 and CON2 depending on the desired constraints. For most models here, two
*MAT_RIGID keywords are used, with the only difference being that one is completely
constrained CON1/CON2 = 7, for the die, and the other allows for movement in one
direction, CON1 = 6 and CON2 = 7, for the punch and blankholder.
For the blank, the simplest model used is 024-PIECEWISE_LINEAR_PLASTICITY
or *MAT_PIECEWISE_LINEAR_PLASTICITY. This requires the same material con-
stants as *MAT_RIGID, here using the corresponding datasheet included in Appendix 8,
as well as a curve for effective stress versus effective plastic strain. This can be defined as
a curve id, or by using at least two of the built in EPS and ES settings. Defining a curve
overrides any other point given in the keyword. The simplest implementation is by defin-
ing two points, making the stress-strain linear. If elastic deformation is not of concern in
the given process, using two points for the plastic region can be good for testing, as it
can easily be changed depending on the focus of the test, and has been used extensively
in this project. This was later used with a defined curve based on processed data from
the tensile tests. One feature of note, is that this model extends the strain-stress curve
beyond the defined strain region, by extending the last two defined points linearly. This
is beneficial for testing, as the material will become proportionally stronger based on the
stress, for any strain or stress values. However, when many datapoints are used for the
curve, extra attention needs to be paid to the last two points, as this will define how break-
age or tearing look. If a curve is near flat at the final points, any stress above this will
present as infinite elongation of the element, which will cause the simulation to terminate.
Since the material in question is highly anisotropic, and since this has large effect on
the process, a material model which can model anisotropy is needed.
The material model was therefore picked using the LS-DYNA material selector.
The
search was limited to, firstly, being applicable to shell elements, secondly, being suit-
able for metals, and thirdly, including anisotropy.
This results in 26 applicable ma-
terial models.
First, those which are made to model failure, damage, surface effects,
specific material, and thermal effects, were discarded, as these factors are not studied
here. Since only the basic material data is available from the data-sheet, as well as the
data which can be obtained from the tensile tests, most of the remaining models require
data input which is not available here. What is left is a few models using Hill’s yield
criterion, and several implementations of Barlat’s yield criterion. Of these, the most ap-
plicable to this process were *MAT_122 or *MAT_HILL_3R for Hill’s, and *MAT_133
or *MAT_BARLAT_YLD2000 for Barlat’s.
Using these on the step 1 model with the same material and model did not shown
any significant differences in resulting geometry or simulation time. Comparing them
68 of 125
==PAGE:75==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
to *MAT_PIECEWISE_LINEAR_PLASTICITY, the effects of anisotropy was clear,
but the relative increase in simulation time was negligible.
The tensile test sample geometries and setup was simulated using the two anisotropy
material models. The differences between the results were negligible in relation to sim-
ulation time, and the variations in results were significantly less than those of similar
individual tensile tests. As this did not prove useful in validating which is the most accu-
rate, and since using the full process geometry was not available for validation purposes
at this time, the choice of material model is based on the theoretical relative accuracy,
as well as previous studies. As described in Section 2.5.2.1, for this process and material,
*MAT_BARLAT_YLD2000 is superior in both these aspects, so this is chosen for all
models going forward[21][22]. An in-depth description of this model is included in section
4.3.6.1.
4.3.6.1
Anisotropy settings and their effects
The BETA option is one of the options on the first required card. This is referred to
as the hardening parameter, and is a value in the range 0 < β < 1. This defines the
ratio between 0 being fully isotropic hardening and 1 being fully kinetic hardening. If a
metal structure is loaded with monotonically increasing load, both kinetic and isotropic
hardening will give the same results. If a structure is under load and then unloaded or
under reverse load, kinematic hardening will give more accurate results. Yielding in kine-
matic hardening will occur at approximately twice the yield stress if the load direction is
reversed. As kinematic hardening is related to the grain directions, the thicker the wall,
the lower the effects of grain directions become[35].
This option can only be ignored if the A setting is negative, which instead enables a
separate card for Chaboche-Roussilier kinematic hardening, requiring significantly more
information about the material. Since obtaining this information wasn’t feasible in this
process, a BETA value is defined.
No default value is defined for the BETA option, no guides indicates what is an ap-
propriate value, and not defining it or setting it to 0 results in the simulation instantly
terminating. Increasing the fraction of kinematic hardening increases the strain hardening
rate, and increases it proportionally to anisotropy, so the effects of anisotropy increases
with amount of kinematic hardening.
Since the thickness is high, in relation to grain related effects, a low value would most
likely be appropriate. In order to test the effect and an appropriate value, identical models
were simulated with BETA = [0.01, 0.1, 0.25, 0.5, 0.9]. For 0.25, 0.5, and 0.9, the blank
broke. This is in line with the increased strain hardening rate lowering the formability.
No effects were noticeable in comparing 0.01 and 0.1. Since completely removing the
effects of kinematic hardening seems unrealistic, a value of 0.1 is chosen. Ideally this
would be tested in relation to what value results in the lowest quantified deviation from
the production sample, but this was not possible. The closest approximation to this is
the finding that increasing the BETA value causes breakage of the workpiece, which isn’t
realistic.
Theoretically this ratio should be increased per step, but since the tendency of the work-
69 of 125
==PAGE:76==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
piece to break increases with each step, this was not done.
The HARD setting is used to define which hardening law is to be used to define the
strain-stress curve. As previously described, the most applicable here is the Voce law[13].
This is defined by setting HARD = 2. This defines the settings K as A, E0 as B, and
N as C. Based on automatic fitting to the datapoints of an averaged curve based on the
tensile tests, the coefficients are determined to be K = 110.10MPa, E0 = 50.84MPa,
and N = 23.38. The fit is included on Figure 31.
Figure 31: Voce law fitted to an average curve based on the tensile test data.
Observing Figure 31, the fit is accurate for the plastic deformation region.
The A setting defines the flow potential exponent.
This takes into account the crys-
tal structure of the metal. The default value is defined as 8, and according to the manual,
8 is recommended for FCC material and 6 for BCC. Since the material used here is an
alloy almost entirely consisting of aluminium, which is an FCC metal, a value of 8 is de-
fined. As previously mentioned, this could be further specified with a negative value if the
Chaboche-Rousselier kinematic hardening parameters are known for the given material.
The yield stress in the three directions are defined by reading from the curves of the
tensile test. The defined values are SIG00 = 53.7, SIG45 = 59.86, and SIG90 = 53.21.
The calculation of the R-values is found in Section 4.1.2, and result in R00 = 0.6605,
R45 = 1.4335, and R90 = 0.4670.
The next important option which need to be defined is AOPT. This defines the rolling di-
rection. Initially this was mistakenly defined as AOPT = 2, meaning globally orthotropic
with material axes determined by vectors. The vector is defined by A1, A2, and A1 below
in card 6. This setting sets the rolling direction to be constant in this direction, meaning
that the model breaks down if any elements rotate such that they are no longer parallel
with the plain. The most optimal solution in this model is AOPT = 0. This defines the
rolling direction locally by the orientation of each element. This means that the rolling
direction needs to be manually rotated using the element editor for all relevant elements.
This can quickly be done by selecting the entire part, and changing their direction based
on a defined vector. This element orientation is also retained when moving to the next
70 of 125
==PAGE:77==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
process, meaning that any changes in grain directions are properly modelled.
For this model, after step 1, the element orientations no longer match that of the rolling
direction. This is to be expected as the material is deformed, reorienting the grains. The
resulting element orientations are however not very realistic, as the element orientation
vectors tend to align themselves with the cardinal directions of the elements. This however
does not seem to be very significant, as the distribution of differently oriented elements
