seem sufficiently homogeneous for the overall material to essentially act isotropic after
step 1. After roughly the halfway point of the step 1 process, most of the elements will
have significantly changed direction relative to their initial direction. The exact effect is
not studied, but the the rolling direction only seem to affect the step 1 simulation results.
This matches the production samples, with only anisotropy related deviations being ob-
served when comparing step 1 simulation results and scans. An example of this direction
change is included on Figure 32.
Figure 32: Example of element direction changes between start of step 1 (left), and start
of step 2 (right).
4.3.7
Springback modelling
In sheet metal forming, the combination of elastic-plastic stretching and bending will
commonly result in significant springback. Since all metals have a modulus of elasticity,
elastic recovery will follow plastic deformation. For large bend angles this will commonly
manifest as positive spring back, and for small bend angles, such as a V-bend, this will
manifest as negative springback.
Predicting this springback is desired to obtain the desired final geometry. When doing
multi-step process simulation, the springback between processes will have an effect on the
the following process, and springback modelling is thus required after each simulation step.
As described, the springback for the process in question is not a significant considera-
tion in production, as the part is allowed to freely deform after being removed from the
punch, as the part can manually be deformed again when positioned on the next step die.
Simulating this movement and these conditions would not be feasible, so the workpiece
is instead imported into a new springback model, where it is allowed to deform. This
deformation can subsequently not be allowed to be free, as it needs to be placed directly
onto the die of the next step. This is done by including the punch geometry of the previ-
ous step, as it’s relevant surfaces are identical to the die of the subsequent step. While it
71 of 125
==PAGE:78==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
would arguably have been more accurate to simulate the process of pushing/pulling the
workpiece onto the next die after springback, this would have added significant time to
the already long simulations, and would also likely result in significant contact problems.
The ways this type of model differs from the other models, is the *CONTROL keywords.
For the control section four keywords are needed. These are:
1. *CONTROL_ACCURACY
2. *CONTROL_IMPLICIT_GENERAL
3. *CONTROL_IMPLICIT_SOLUTION
4. *CONTROL_TERMINATION
The settings for *CONTROL_ACCURACY used here are OSU = 1, INN = 2, and
IACC = 1. Since this is a very short simulation with no defined movement, these set the
accuracy of the simulation to be high.
The non-default settings for *CONTROL_IMPLICIT_GENERAL are IMFLAG = 1,
and DT0 = 1, in order to run implicit with a large initial timestep. This could also be
done using the explicit method with IMFLAG = 2, but this is not attempted here.
For *CONTROL_IMPLICIT_SOLUTION, the only non-default setting is DCTOL =
1e-06.
For *CONTROL_TERMINATION, the endtime should be defined as ENDTIM = 1, or
the same as the first time-step, to ensure a single step, as this is sufficient for springback.
4.3.8
General observations and process description
The first step of the process caused significant problems, taking several months of trou-
bleshooting before a sufficient result could be attained. Using the model developed for
step 1, the subsequent steps required less troubleshooting and debugging in order to attain
a sufficiently accurate finished part. The fundamental parts of the model were studied
and established based on the step 1 model, but the following steps required significant
alterations to the physical variables, such as friction.
4.3.8.1
Step 1
Based on the experience gained from the initial example geometries, and their model set-
tings, the first models of step 1 were quarter-geometries running implicit. The meshes of
the tools and blank were of the same size, and uniform across the part for study. The mate-
rial models were the simplest applicable model *MAT_PIECEWISE_LINEAR_PLASTI-
CITY or MAT_024, using a two point line. These value were based on experience gained
from the initial example models. This model had proved to be effective, and changing it
significantly to study the effects of material strength is easy and quick. The other material
constants were based on the provided datasheet shown in appendix 8.
The first attempts were using 8mm meshes, but this resulted in the model not reach-
ing convergence. Switching to a 4mm had the same outcome. Going down to a 1mm
72 of 125
==PAGE:79==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
mesh, the punch could move approximately a third of its total travel before convergence
issues. Running with such a fine however required simulation times exceeding 4 days.
This setup can be seen on Figures 33 and 34.
Figure 33: Initial simulation geometry for step 1, showing the top at t = 0.
The geometry seen on Figure 33 was developed from creating an assembly of the produc-
tion parts which would come into contact with the blank, and reducing it to the surfaces
pointing towards the blank.
Figure 34: Initial simulation geometry for step 1, showing the bottom at the final time-
step before termination (303/1000).
Figure 34 shows the approximate state at which they would all error terminate.
No
clear reason can be seen.
The effective stress (Von Mises) is in the range of [0.853 ·
10−3MPa, 0.209 · 10−3MPa], with no clear local peaks, clearly not indicating any prob-
lems.
73 of 125
==PAGE:80==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
Figure 35: Initial simulation edge thickness, showing only the blank at the final time-step
before termination (303/1000).
Looking at the edge thickness shown on Figure 36, the most extreme local thinning is very
light, and the previous 10 time-steps show approximately the same, so its not spontaneous.
Furthermore, none of the thicknesses exceed the here fixed blankholder gap of 1.18mm or
the punch-die gap of 1.20mm. The only location which might be concerning is two nodes
of the blank near the edge of the punch tip, deforming in an unexpected manner as shown
on Figure 36.
Figure 36: Initial simulation edge thickness, showing only the blank at the final time-step
before termination (303/1000).
This behaviour is present on some of the model. This clearly indicates contact problems.
However, this type of deformation do not occur near the time of failure, does not get
worse over time, and does not correlated to any type of movement across any the at-
tempted simulations. It could also indicate that the time-steps are too large, meaning
that too much is happening between steps. Lowering the time steps did however not fix
the problem, even when lowered to the theoretical limit, and neither did any changes to
the contact model. The only defining factor of when and where this occurs was the mesh
size, but with no correlation between mesh size and failure time.
After much troubleshooting, the problem turned out to be a combination of time-step
74 of 125
==PAGE:81==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
size and contact formulation. Since lowering the time-step beyond a certain point would
be infeasible, the speed of the movement can be used to compensate.
Since directly
lowering it would have the same effect, varying the speed is the only possibility. Since
problems occur in the contact formulation, it can be tied to the contact formulation
through the viscous dampening coefficient, as described in detail in Section 4.3.4.1. Using
a very high viscous dampening, the punch movement effectively stops being linear, despite
how its defined by the curve used in *BOUNDARY_PRESCRIBED_MOTION_RIGID.
While the punch does move linearly, the force applied to the workpiece elements vary.
This approach also requires a very soft contact model. The one that ended up working
was *CONTACT_FORMING_SURFACE_TO_SURFACE_MORTAR with the corre-
sponding soft constraint option SOFT=4. This approach is however not recommended,
as the forces and displacements fluctuate to an extreme degree, as the effective punch
movement constantly accelerates and decelerates based on encountered contact difficul-
ties. However, this was the only way of making the step 1 process progress.
After a significant effort was made in optimizing the model, the viscous dampening co-
efficient could be lowered to VDC = 50, and a more fitting soft constraint option could
be used, namely the default penalty formulation. While this made the effective punch
movement almost completely linear, the results varied heavily, as can be seen from two of
the resulting step 1 geometries shown on Figure 37.
(a) Resulting final geometry from running im-
plicit, using a 1mm blank mesh.
(b) Resulting final geometry from running im-
plicit, using a 0.5mm blank mesh.
Figure 37: Resulting final geometry from running implicit.
As can be seen from Figure 37, the results vary extremely between even fine mesh sizes,
and the problem of shooting nodes deforming the surface as shown on Figure 36 only gets
worse throughout the process. It should be noted that this is with shooting node logic
turned off. All meshes coarser than 1mm show the same extreme local thinning as seen
on Figure 37a. This is not just thinning, but the blank tearing. The model showed here
is just one which tore just as the process finished. If it happens any earlier the simulation
will fail to achieve convergence and terminate. While it is shown on 37b that the 0.5mm
mesh does finish without fully tearing, it is clearly very close at several stages of the
process, only managing to move forward due to the smaller mesh allowing for the local
stress peaks to be distributed between more elements, aided by the the infinitely linear
stress-strain curve. As explained in Section 4.3.6, this is because this keyword extends
the last two-point line. It should thus be noted that using an approximation of a real
strain-stress curve, or even a slightly steeper or more gradual curve causes it to fail as
75 of 125
==PAGE:82==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
shown on 37a. Material models including anisotropy was also attempted. Changing the
anisotropy constants had little effect in relation to overcoming the problems, and since
these define the strain-stress relationship as functions, the effects were similar to using
a defined curve. This however also showed that the simulation time or stability was not
significantly affected by using a more advanced material model.
Several attempts were made at studying the effect of friction.
This was done in the
same manner as the previously described convergence studies. The only constant of these
was that the model would either fail immediately or fail very late if the friction coeffi-
cient approached zero. None of these showed any correlation or convergence, except for
arguably one attempted on the final implicit model used for 37b.
Figure 38: Punch force as a function of time for various friction coefficients used in
identical model, only including those which resulted in normal termination.
Observing Figure 38, it can be seen firstly that any friction coefficients over µ = 0.1 re-
sulted in early termination, that the force curves are extremely unstable for all frictions,
and that the lower the friction coefficient, the further into the process it gets before the
contact model completely breaks down.
Since no settings could be changed to achieve a better results, the only possible solu-
tion was to remake the model to use the explicit method, given the time constraints.
Switching to using the explicit model, it is clear that this is more suitable for this process,
as the extensive contact problems makes convergence close to impossible for an implicit
scheme. Using the explicit method for such a model exposed several other advantages.
The smaller time-steps of the explicit model is significantly more effective in limiting the
contact problems. The fact that it does not need to converge, but rather continues until
a problem has become bad enough that it cant obtain energy balance, makes it much
easier to determine the cause of problems. After switching the relevant keywords, the
explicit models could in most cases run until the designated termination time. Generally
the simulation time was also decreased significantly, being less than half their implicit
counterparts.The need for very soft contacts was also no longer present. This however
exposed several major problems.
76 of 125
==PAGE:83==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
As the flange thickens, much of its thickness increases beyond the thickness of the gap.
This can also be observed on the implicit results from Figure 37, where the edge thickness
increases to t > 1.3mm for most of the circumference, despite the gap being fixed at
1.18mm. This was previously hypothesised to be an insignificant side effect of the softer
contact, as the much softer contact models effectively increased the gap, by allowing a
larger amount of surface penetration before the contact force would start to have an effect.
This thickening however, caused the friction force to become so high that the workpiece
would tear. No matter what the friction coefficient was set to, tearing would occur, even if
increasing the allowed penetration beyond reasonable limits. Studying this effect resulted
in the graph shown on Figure 39.
Figure 39: Punch force as a function of time for varying friction coefficients used in
otherwise identical models.
Observing Figure 39 it is clear that the punch force is directly tied to the friction factor,
which is to be expected as they are the two main opposite force components. As a higher
friction increases the draw-in force, the tendency of the workpiece to tear should disappear
with lower friction. This is however not fully the case here. The drops in force around
s = 6 indicates when a tear starts. This does also occur for µ = 0.05, although not before
t = 7s. The times at which the peaks start are similar, and these indicate when the
thickening has become sufficient to start locking inside the gap. As this starts happening,
the zero friction model is unable to obtain energy balance and starts breaking down. This
point is constant for all friction coefficients, as is to be expected, as the thickening up
till that point is almost solely due to the radial stress caused by the sharp decrease in
circumference.
As the curves also indicate, no friction value is low enough to stop the tearing, while
maintaining energy balance, meaning resulting in early error termination. Even when
allowing significant penetration, the thickening is too extreme to be overcome.
Some
examples of this is included on Figure 40.
77 of 125
==PAGE:84==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
(a) Resulting geometry from running explicit,
using µ = 0.1 for all contacts.
(b) Resulting geometry from running explicit,
using µ = 0.05 for all contacts.
Figure 40: Resulting geometry from running explicit with constant gap.
From observing the differences in Figure 40, changing the friction coefficient does not
solve the problem. Since the production material had been received at this point, the
material model was using the valued obtained from the tensile tests described in Section
4.1.2, so using too weak a material could be ruled out. While lowering the friction does
decrease the stress, solving the problem of the thickening required more fundamental
changes. Increasing the strength of the material was attempted, but the required increase
had to be far beyond what could be considered realistic. While the contact model was
already allowing for significant thickening beyond the gap size, as seen on Figure 40, with
the edge thickness exceeding 1.3mm despite the gap being 1.18mm, so the only remaining
option was changing the size of the gap between the blankholder and die. Continually
increasing the gap until total breakage could be prevented resulted in Figure 41.
Figure 41: Finished workpiece geometry from using a gap of 3.8mm.
As can be seen on Figure 41, increasing the gap by 222% prevents breakage for all but
the last elements of the edge. As the flange thickening is not constrained for longer when
using a larger gap, the edge thickness increase is elevated, ensuring the breakage, just at
78 of 125
==PAGE:85==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
a later stage of the process. For this large a gap, the blank does not get stuck in the
die-blankholder gap, but rather gets stuck in the punch-blankholder gap. This effectively
means that even if the flange can get out of the horizontal gap, it will get stuck in the
vertical gap. With horizontal referring to the plane perpendicular to the punch move-
ment direction. The only possible solution to this would be stopping the process before
the edge enters the vertical gap, but this would result in an edge thickness far beyond
what can be measured on the production samples, as well as wrinkling. It can also be
observed that increasing the gap allows for more wrinkling. The wrinkling of the flange
using the 3.8mm gap is so significant, that it can seen on the wall even after entering the
punch-blankholder gap.
An attempt was made at stopping the process before the edge entered the blankholder-
punch gap.
However, when using this step 1 geometry in the step 2 simulation, the
excessively thick lower part of the wall would immediately cause it to break, as it entered
the new blankholder gap.
These results, combined with no evidence of wrinkling on the production part, lead to the
conclusion that a fixed gap cannot result in the production sample geometry. Even when
considering factors not possible to include in the simulation, such a large discrepancy
does not seem possible. At this point the most plausible scenario is that the production
samples were made using a variable force controlled gap. This would prevent excessive
thickening throughout the process, as well as wrinkling, while still feasibly keeping the
draw-in stress from exceeding the ultimate yield strength of the material. All further
models were therefore changed to use variable gap.
The variable gap was introduced by removing the y-axis constraint of the blankholder,
and introducing a force load on the part. While the gap had been specified as fixed, the
force the press exerts on the blankholder was specified as being 250t in the production. As
step 1 and 2 are in the same press, and since this is a quarter model, that would equate to
307kN. Using this force for the blankholder resulted in blank being completely stuck, and
breaking immediately. A movement constraint was thus introduced to the blankholder.
The production specified that shims would prevent the blankholder gap from decreasing
past 90% of the initial blank thickness, meaning a decrease of 0.28mm from 1.18mm.
The optimal method for constraining the movement would be introducing a part which
can physically prevent the movement. This can also be done with keywords, but the only
one applicable here would be *CONSTRAINED_RIGID_BODY_STOPPERS. The lat-
ter option was chosen. Due to the significant force on the blankholder, and the due to the
nature of contacts between shell elements, the blankholder will immediate move through
the blank in the first step of the process, as the contact does not update frequently
enough to catch it, or if it does, the high velocity of the blankholder will cause the re-
action forces to make it shoot off in the opposite direction. The method which prevents
this is by limiting the velocity of the part. As this also requires the keyword *CON-
STRAINED_RIGID_BODY_STOPPERS, its options to limit displacement is used as
well.
When constraining the movement to ±0.28mm however does not have an effect on the
result of the process, as having such a high force essentially makes it a fixed gap at the
79 of 125
==PAGE:86==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
lower end of the constraint, making the gap 0.9mm throughout the process. The result
of this is shown on Figure 42.
Figure 42: Resulting geometry using constrained variable gap and full specified force.
Figure 42 shows clear and early tearing of both sides, meaning that this blankholder force
is not usable. Several models were then made using smaller blankholder force. It was
found that if the blankholder force was reduced to be approximately a tenth the force,
the process would almost result in no breakage. From discussions with the production
engineers it was discovered that at the last 10mm of the punch travel, the force was
reduced to 35t, as the flange area in the gap decreases. As the punch travel, according to
the CAD, exceeds total flange removal by more than 10mm, this was instead interpreted
as the point at which approximately 10mm of the flange remained in the gap. Using this
with the lowered end pressure could result in no breakage. However, the flange edge still
thickened to just above 1.3mm, meaning it would break when trying to enter the vertical
blankholder-punch gap. Testing [100t, 75t, 50t, 25t, and 12.5t], all models except for
12.5t resulted in a similar result to Figure 43.
80 of 125
==PAGE:87==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
Figure 43: Resulting geometry using constrained variable gap and 25t blankholder pres-
sure (61.312kN due to quarter model).
As Figure 43 shows, the long radius side is tearing. How far into the process tearing occurs
is only slightly dependent on the blankholder force, only varying by at most 5 elements
from what can be seen on Figure 43. Decreasing the force beyond a tenth of the specified
force increased the resulting edge thickness. Increasing it slightly to 12.742t, as this would
be an even 125kN, the flange could become slightly thinner without breaking. Consider-
ing that this seems to be the optimal force, that the press is specified in 10 · kN, and that
a misplaced decimal is a common human error, it strongly indicates that 12.5kN is the
optimal and true production blankholder force. This force also correlates with the theo-
retical calculations of Section 2.1. Furthermore, looking at the force curves of the punch,
these generally correlate with being a quarter of the theoretical punch force of Section 2.1.
Extensive testing was performed in order to try and reduce the flange edge thickness
to a point at which it wouldn’t break when entering the vertical blankholder-punch gap.
Increasing or decreasing the friction beyond µ = 0.1 would decrease or increase gap size
respectively, as more or less friction force would be countering the blankholder force,
causing the gap to stay at one of the blankholder movement constraints. Since either of
these configurations result in failure, the blankolder limits were tested, as the gap would
vary throughout the process. This seemed to only increase the chance of failure, with the
exception of increasing the limits to ±0.3mm, which did cause the edge to become ever
so slightly thinner.
Last option was changing the mesh size. Decreasing it from 2mm to 1mm decreased
the edge thickness slightly, but also increased the chance of instability, and thus error ter-
mination. As previously explained, this is due to increased differences in node numbers
in contacts between tool and workpiece, increasing the effect of nodes excessively pen-
etrating. After significant testing, The tool mesh was refined, which reduced the effect
slightly. The final change which solved the problem was lowering the maximum allowed
81 of 125
==PAGE:88==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
velocity of the blankholder from an initial limit of 5mm
s
to 1mm
s . This limit can increase
throughout the process, but needs to be low for the first half of the process. While it
decreases accuracy, as there is no such limit in reality, it is essential to preventing error
termination. The resulting blankholder movement is included in Figure 44.
Figure 44: Blankholder displacement represented as gap size throughout the process.
Figure 44 shows the gap decreasing until it reaches the 1mm thick blank. It then slowly
increases as the flange thickens. It increases to the initial gap size, after which it drops
quickly due to the flange leaving the die-Blankholder gap.
The only remaining problem is the elongation of the long radius wall. This was somewhat
remedied by altering the rolling direction used in the anisotropy model. This is further
investigated in Section 4.1.1, directly shown on Figure 19, and the exact material model
settings described in Section 4.3.6.1.
While this was the optimal possible model at the time, optimizations made on later
stages meant that the step 1 model could be reduced to using a 0.5mm mesh for the
blank. The exact optimization was lowering the BSORT value, as explained in Section
4.3.4.4. This is the final step 1 model, and is explained in detail in Section 4.3.8.11, with
its results presented in Section 4.3.8.12.
4.3.8.2
Final step 1 model setup
The conclusion of step 1 results in the following model.
The punch movement is defined using one *BOUNDARY_PRESCRIBED_MOTION_R-
IGID defining displacement in the y-axis using one *DEFINE_CURVE consisting of four
points.
A1 [s] (Time)
0.0
0.1
6.0
6.1
O1 [mm] (Displacement)
0.0
0.0
201.0
20.0
82 of 125
==PAGE:89==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
All blank nodes on the two symmetry planes are constrained using two *BOUNDARY_S-
PC_SET, one for each defined node-sets.
The blankholder movement is constrained using one *CONSTRAINED_RIGID_BO-
DY_STOPPERS, defined by a minimum and maximum relative displacement of −0.3mm
and 0.3mm respectively using two *DEFINE_CURVE, relative to a fixed part, which here
is the die.
The contact model consists of three *CONTACT_AUTOMATIC_SURFACE_TO_SUR-
FACE for blank-die, blank-punch, and blank-blankholder. All three are completely iden-
tical exept for the part ids.
MPR = 1 to output the reaction forces on the tool. The static coefficient of friction
is set to FS = 0.1, the coefficient for viscous friction is set to VC = 46.2MPa as described
in Section 4.3.4, and the viscous damping coefficient is set to VDC = 10. The small
penetration in contact search option is turned on PENCHK=1.
All scale factors are set to 1, except for the slave and master penalty stiffness. These
are set to SFS = 3 and SFM = 3. For this model it is essential to have a high penalty
stiffness to prevent nodes from penetrating too much. While it limits the allowed thick-
ening to more realistic values, it’s main function is to prevent contact instability. Since
step 1 has a high amount of nodes inside the blankholder-die gap, this model requires the
highest value. Alternately, if these scaling factors are set any higher, the blank will break
from not being allowed to thicken, increasing friction.
AB is ticked, to allow for cards 9 and 10. The only changes from default is the BSORT
being set to BSORT = 2.
For the control section 5 keywords are needed. The first is *CONTROL_CONTACT.
While it is more important for the later steps, the initial penetration check in contact
surfaces is turned on with ISLCHK = 1. This is to ensure stability as all tools except for
the blankholder start in contact with the blank. Next the settings SHLTHK = 2. and
THKCHG = 1 are needed to consider shell thickness in contacts. No other settings are
required here.
The next is *CONTROL_HOURGLASS, which is needed to prevent hourglassing. Here
the setting IHQ = 8 is used since the shell elements are fully integrated. Changing the
hourglass coefficient from the default QH = 0.1 is not advised.
The keyword *CONTROL_SHELL is needed to define the use of shell elements. The
only non-default value here is the use of full sorting of triangular elements ESORT = 1
for stability, ISTUPD = 1 to define that shell thickness will change based on membrane
straining, and CNTCO = 1 to define that the shell reference surface offset is based on the
shell thickness for the contacts. It can be beneficial to increase WRPANG = 20, which
defines the element warpage angle at which it will start showing errors in the output, as
the output log will inevitably show quite a few error messages as it is almost impossible
to prevent significant element warpage for any of these steps.
83 of 125
==PAGE:90==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
The keyword *CONTROL_TERMINATION is need for defining the termination time,
which is set at 6s here. The exact process time is not known, but it is an estimation
based on force curves defined by readings of the press force gauges. As described in Sec-
tion 4.3.1, it is often beneficial to define the other termination conditions, but since this
is a highly optimized model, none of these are needed here.
The last control keyword is *CONTROL_TIMESTEP, which is needed for defining the
mass scaling, which is set to DT2MS = -3e-05. Increasing it to DT2MS = -5e-05 to
decrease simulation time can be beneficial as it has no significant effects here, but as its
the final model, ensuring more accurate results is desirable.
For the DATABASE cards the included cards are *DATABASE_ASCII_option *DATA-
BASE_BINARY_D3PLOT and *DATABASE_EXTENT_BINARY. As these all define
the output and its accuracy, this is all dependent on what is desired. For the time, 6s,
and model size, 143459, nodes used here, it should be noted that an output frequency of
DT = 0.1 produces 4.5Gb of output. If the workpiece is needed for a subsequent step, as
it is here, the correct number of shell integration points in the output needs to be defined
in *DATABASE_EXTENT_BI- NARY.
All the defined curves are explained for the previous keywords, but it can be useful
to define the movements with *DEFINE_CURVE_SMOOTH if one does not desire to
manually smooth movement changes, or if the defined movement is too abrupt for the
contact model despite defining stepped changes in *DEFINE_CURVE.
For outputting the workpiece two interface keywords are needed.
According to the
documentation only one of these are needed, but if both are not included here, the
workpiece will not be part of the output.
The first is *INTERFACE_SPRINGBA-
CK_LSDYNA, and only needs the part id defined if the second keyword *INTER-
FACE_SPRINGBACK_LSDYNA_THICKNESS is included. The second keyword also
needs the part id, and a defined output type. This is here FTYPE = 3, as the LSDA
format creates a file which acts as a keyword file, making it easy to import. For unknown
reasons the setting NSHV also needs to be defined as non-zero for proper function, but
the value chosen did not appear to have any effect throughout its extensive usage here,
and is set to NSHV = 1.
For the blankholder load, it can be applied in multiple ways, and since it is used for a rigid
tool which is constrained in all but one displacement DOF in this case, the mechanism only
matters in relation to simplicity. Several of the loading keywords have been used, by the
total force or pressure applied and its directions can be confusing when troubleshooting. If
the part is not fully rigid, *LOAD_NODE_SET have shown itself to work for this type of
model, and if the load needs to be on a non flat surface, *LOAD_SEGMENT_SET and
*LOAD_SEGMENT_SET_NONUNIFORM has also been used to some success here,
although it should be noted that they use pressure and not force, which can make deter-
mining the exact applied force troublesome, and increases the possibility of making errors
related to consistent units. For simplicity, a single *LOAD_RIGID_BODY is used here.
This keyword applies a force load equally between all nodes of a part. It requires the part
ID, the load direction, which is DOF = 2 y-direction in this case, a load curve id, and
a scaling factor depending on the direction, either SF = 1 for positive or SF = −1 for
84 of 125
==PAGE:91==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
negative relative to axis direction. The final load curve is defined in Table 3.
A1 [s] (Time)
0.0
0.01
4.0
4.1
6.0
6.1
O1 [N] (Force)
0.0
31250
31250
8750
8750
8750
Table 3: Blankholder force curve.
When defining the loads it is essential to start at zero to prevent instability, and can
in some rare cases cause problems at the end if it is not extended slightly beyond the
termination time. Additionally, the point at which contact is made between parts, the
load should be small and gradually increased from this point.
For the materials, two *MAT_RIGID are needed for the tools. As previously described
the material constants are defined by the tool material, here being Uddeholm Sleipner[34].
Two are needed for having one which is constrained in all directions for the die, and one
which allows for y-direction movement for the blankholder and punch.
As previously
described that constitutes using CMO = 1, CON1 = 7, and CON2 = 7, with the other
using CON1 = 6 instead.
For the blank material one keyword of *MAT_BARLAT_YLD2000 is used.
The re-
quired settings are extensive, and can be found in Section 4.3.6.
Two *SECTION_SHELL keywords are needed. One for the tools and one for the work-
piece. For the tools the element formulation should be ELFORM = 2 for rigid shell tools,
and this will also automatically be changed by LS-DYNA, if for example fully integrated
elements are chosen instead. The number of integration points does benefit from a slight
increase for contact stability, and is set to NIP = 3. The location of reference surface for
three dimensional shell elements, or essentially the shell thickness direction, should be set
to NLOC = 1 for the tools. This ensures that the surfaces of the tools do not need to
be offset before assembling the geometry, and also ensures that the surface normals point
towards the workpiece. This should be checked and ensured for all tool surfaces, as not
doing this is very likely to cause contact problems. Regarding the shell thickness T1, it
is only relevant in regards to non-rigid tools, and the contact formulation. Larger thick-
nesses is undesirable in relation to it allowing for more penetration, lowering the accuracy
of the model, but should also be sufficiently large to prevent total node penetration. For
this model, the optimal setting has been determined to be T1 = 1mm. For the blank the
optimal element formulation has been determined to be ELFORM = 16 fully integrated
shell elements. Theoretically formulation 25 and 26 should be more fitting as they are
better at handling thickness stretch in shear, but it has not been possible to successfully
implement these in this project. For the number of integration points, any value above
NIP = 5 does not significantly affect the model or its accuracy, except for slight increase
in simulation time. The thickness is defined as 1mm based on the CAD, and the direction
is defined NLOC = 0 as this has proven to be the most stable condition when both sides
are in contact, as it is here in both the horizontal and vertical gaps. The only downside to
this, is that the thickness offset is not represented graphically, making node penetration
in the contacts harder to detect.
A *PART_PART keyword is needed for each part, and the corresponding material and
section ids need to be defined for all, as the model will refuse to run if this is not the case.
85 of 125
==PAGE:92==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
This concludes the final model, and the relevant keyword code can be found in Appendix
8.
4.3.8.3
Final step 1 simulation results
The final resulting geometry of step 1 is presented here.
(a)
(b)
Figure 45: (a) Scan result for reference. (b) Final step 1 blank geometry, showing the
resulting edge thickness after last step.
Starting with a thickness map, which can be observed on Figure 45, the thickness range
86 of 125
==PAGE:93==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
of [0.941mm, 1.273mm] is approximately equal to that of the scans [0.86mm, 1.27mm],
or discounting the lower limit caused by post-processing errors, [≈0.95mm, ≈1.27mm].
The thickness distribution is however not equal to the scans. Much of the simulation thick-
ness is concentrated near the edge, with the majority of the part surface being < 1mm.
While the pattern of having approximately uniform thickness from approximately half of
the wall height and up is similar, the scans show it being in a range of [1.00mm, 1.10mm],
where as the simulations show most of it being in a range of [0.95mm, 1.00mm]. This
means that, since the average wall heights do not differ significantly, the simulation part
either experiences heavier thickening in the flange, or that the friction is higher than in
the production, causing the draw-in stress to be higher in the simulation.
The simulation results also fail to show the slight thinning near the punch rounding,
as the theory of stress distribution would also predict as an area of necking. This could
be explained by the elements near the rounding having increased stiffness, due to being
warped as they cover the rounding. Evidence of this can be observed near the long ra-
dius, where more thinning occurs, showing a line of thicker elements in the region where
necking should theoretically occur. It should be noted here that the long radius is the
right side of Figure 45, with the short radius being the left.
The thickness regions near the short radius are distributed almost exactly as the scans
show, with the exception of the edge thickness.
In the scans, the edge of the flange
thickens to > 1.2mm near both the short and long radius edges, with the middle being
thinner, but in the simulation, almost the entire edge thickening above 1.2mm occur near
the long radius edge. The cause of this is that the long radius wall length is higher than
in the production part, effectively moving more of the thickening towards the long radius.
As discussed in depth in Section 4.1.1, this is most likely due to the simulation being
limited to using uniform friction between parts. The excessive edge thickening near the
long radius then in turn causes the draw in stress to increase locally, causing the region
of excessive thinning further up the wall. In retrospect, since this seems to be the largest
discrepancy, it could possibly be remedied by splitting the punch into several parts to
have more contact keywords with differing friction factors, but the accompanying contact
issues would require significant effort. It could also be remedied by altering the anisotropy
constants of the material, but as this could affect more than just the desired wall length,
it is not attempted here.
87 of 125
==PAGE:94==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
Figure 46: Blankholder displacement represented as gap size throughout the process for
the final model.
Observing figure 46, the gap does not decrease as quickly as seen on Figure 44, which is
most likely due to the blank bending up slightly from the punch contact. As the blank is
pulled in and starts deforming, this effect disappears, but not before the flange thickness
has increased slightly. After this, it follows the same pattern as Figure 44, increasing
over the coarse of the process as the flange thickens, then hitting the lower limit after the
flange leaves the gap.
Figure 47: Final step 1 blank geometry, showing the resulting effective strain.
88 of 125
==PAGE:95==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
It should here be noted that effective plastic strain is a monotonically increasing scalar
value which is calculated incrementally as a function of (Dp)ij, the plastic component
of the rate of deformation tensor[36]. As can be observed from Figure 47, the majority
of the strain is localized to the region of the workpiece corresponding to the excessive
edge thickening. As this distribution is heavily dependent on the slight inaccurate edge
thickening, it will naturally be problematic for when the part is transferred to the next
step.
(a) Step 1 at the halfway point of the simulation process.
(b) Step 1 at the end of the simulation.
Figure 48: Half and final step 1 blank geometry, showing the resulting effective stress
(Von Mises).
Observing Figure 48, most of the stresses are constantly in the range of approximately
[90MPa −125MPa], corresponding to the plastic region of the material’s stress-strain
curve. As the process progresses, the stress of the surface on the punch nose drops. As
89 of 125
==PAGE:96==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
described in Section 2.1, the workpiece at the punch end should experience tensile stress
corresponding to the draw-in stress throughout the process, but this does not seem to
be the case. This is likely due to the deviation from not being axisymmetric, making
it stiffer near the punch nose. As the stress seems to increase with decreasing distance
to the edge, it somewhat corresponds with the previous hypothesis, that the friction in
the punch-blankholder gap might increase due to the workpiece warping locally, as in
springback, increasing the normal pressure and thus friction force.
It should be noted, that the scales on Figure 48 are much higher than the stresses shown.
This is due to some elements locking throughout the process, and retaining those higher
stress values. Since this phenomenon appears as approximately 10 to 30 elements scat-
tered throughout the process, and throughout the 125, 057 elements of the blank, they
are almost impossible to spot.
Figure 49: Final step 1 blank geometry after springback analysis showing the resulting
effective stress (Von Mises).
Observing the stress after springback simulation on Figure 48, the stress does not seem
to decrease, but rather migrates up from the edge and towards the short radius side.
The cause of this is, as explained in Section 4.3.7, that the part tries to deform, by the
short radius wall bending inwards and the long radius wall bending outwards. Since this
cannot be allowed for fitting the workpiece onto the step 2 tools, the springback simulation
includes the punch, meaning that very little physical springback is possible, thus limiting
the amount of residual stress that can be removed.
4.3.8.4
Step 2
Initially the model used in step 1 was directly transferred to the new tool geometry, but as
a deformed part, meaning the transferred step 1 blank, has to be inserted into the model,
the first part of step 2 is figuring out how to manage the insertion without penetration.
90 of 125
==PAGE:97==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
Luckily the step 2 die has the same geometry as the step 1 punch in this case, so simu-
lating springback on the punch fixes the radial penetration. Since a side-effect of using
explicit is that the dynamic nature of the model causes the flat surface of the blank to
deform as it accelerates and decelerates, this surface has to be seperated from the punch.
The final method of inserting the blank was to move the blankholder up, and then have
it clamp down onto the blank. Since high velocities causes contact issues, this clamping
adds 1s to the start of the process.
After obtaining the first viable blank in step 1, attempts were made at moving on to
step 2. These immediately broke, tearing the blank as soon as the punch made contact.
Assuming element locking, since the initial step 2 tool geometries were still using uniform
meshes, these were refined based on the experiences gained in the step 1 simulation pro-
cess. While the tools were refined multiple times, the final tool geometry is included in
Figure 50.
(a) Step 2 tool geometry top view.
(b) Step 2 tool geometry section view.
Figure 50: Final step 2 tool geometry.
The first blank which was transferred over used a 4mm mesh. As updating the tool ge-
ometry still resulted in instant fracture at punch contact, this step had to wait until a
2mm meshed blank was available from step 1.
Using a 2mm mesh blank, the process did not immediately break at punch contact,
but very soon after. The solution to this was increasing the blankholder gap, as it was
fixed. To force the process to move forward, the gap had to be increased by several mil-
limiters. This however resulted in very significant wrinkling inside the gap, locking and
subsequently tearing the blank. Since no amount of variable changes could change this a
variable gap was implemented as in step 1. The best possible results of using a fixed gap
of 1.2mm, as described by the production CAD, are included in Figure 51.
91 of 125
==PAGE:98==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
(a) Initial step 2 attempt with fixed blankholder
gap.
(b) Step 2 model using variable gap.
Figure 51: Resulting geometry from first attempts at step 2 simulation.
This was implemented in a similar manner to the step 1 model. The problem with this is
that the constraint keyword *CONSTRAINED_RIGID_BODY_STOPPERS only works
relative to the initial part location, regardless of birth time of the constraint, which is in
conflict with the required movement for clamping. This can be overcome by placing the
blankholder such that the gap is nominal initially, then quickly moving the blankholder
up after simulation start, setting the contact birth time to just after this movement,
then performing the clamping motion, setting the death time of the movement *BOUND-
ARY_PRESCRIBED_MOTION_RIGID to just after clamping, and the birth time of
the constraint to this time as well. If the movement is active, the part is locked at the
prescribed position, so its important to deactivate this movement. Subsequently, if there
is a gap between the end of the rigid movement and the constraint, then the velocity is
unconstrained, causing the contact between the blank and blankholder to break. It is
important to note here, that these keywords are not made inactive by their defined curves
as starting or ending at specified times, so it is not possible simple to set the movement
curves to end after clamping, but needs to be defined by the DEATH option in the card.
The resulting blankholder displacement curve is included in Table 4.

Table 4: Displacement curve for blankholder clamping over blank.
For Table 4, the same precautions should be taken as described for the step 1 punch.
If the relative velocity or acceleration is too high when contact is made or ended, then
contact instability will occur and result in error termination. This is also the case for
the step 2 punch displacement, requiring a more gradual curve for the contact, as it is no
longer two parallel surfaces coming into contact as it was in step 1.
The force is applied in a similar manner to step 1, and the amount of force is also similar,
92 of 125
==PAGE:99==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
as step 1 and step 2 are in one press.
While changing to a force controlled gap made the process much more stable and en-
abled it to move forward, attaining a non-torn blank requires very specific conditions,
and finding these required significant troubleshooting.
Some of the common failure mechanisms using this setup are included on Figures 52-
54.
(a) Common failure mechanism 1.
(b) Common failure mechanism 2.
Figure 52: Example of common failure mechanisms.
The gap and its movement pattern over time is tied to the friction, as the blankholder
force is countered by the friction force, the movement constraints in both directions, the
velocity constraint, the material thickening, including increased local thickening from ele-
ments locking, wrinkles transferring radial stress as force pushing the blankholder up, and
the contact settings such as the stiffness. The effects of these are tied, as some can cause
the gap to change at different points of the process, or combining to create a separate
effect, making finding the correct settings essentially trial and error. The problem with
this process is that only very specific settings result in an intact blank of approximately
desired thickness.
The failures seen on Figure 52 commonly result from having the lower displacement con-
straint too low, setting the friction too low, or the blankholder force too high. In Figure
52a the gap decreases too much, locking any further movement, and tearing at the edge
along the end of the blankholder-die gap. Using too coarse a mesh for either the tool or
blank will also fail in a similar manner. A result like Figure 52b is common if the gap is
either not allowed to open enough for the thicker edge to pass through, or if the friction
is too low, causing the gap to decrease to the point of locally thick elements breaking. As
the thickness near the edge is not uniform, the blankholder pressure is distributed along
fewer elements, causing the stress around these elements to exceed the fracture point.
93 of 125
==PAGE:100==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
(a) Common failure mechanism 3.
(b) Common failure mechanism 4.
Figure 53: Example of common failure mechanism
The failures seen on Figure 53 are common with element locking, often due to excessive
wrinkling. These both present as a very small amount of elements getting stuck and its
thickness increasing heavily, observed as red spots, with blue spots beside them showing
the resulting tear. If the gap is allowed to increase to a point where wrinkling is possible,
if the wrinkles are very small relative to the elements, then the warping will cause them
to lock, causing these very local tears. This can be tricky to troubleshoot, as they are
not visible without looking closely at the elements. Looking at the high reaction forces,
wrinkling will not seem likely, despite these being caused by the wrinkling. It also does
not happen when the wrinkles are slightly larger, as the individual element warping is
lower, leading to the possibly incorrect conclusion that it must be caused by the elements
locking due to the blankholder force being too high.
(a) Common failure mechanism 5.
(b) Common failure mechanism 6.
Figure 54: Example of common failure mechanism
The failures seen on Figure 53 are common with the force becoming too high at critical
parts of the process. If the friction force decreases then the gap will close too much, lock-
ing first the thickest point and then tearing across the rest, as the force will be transferred
94 of 125
==PAGE:101==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
to the remaining intact elements. Since the friction has an upper limit in the form of the
coefficient for viscous friction VDC, if contact is limited to a region of higher thickness,
the friction force decreases. At the same time, if VDC is too high or not defined, the local
friction can become too great causing the gap to open enough for wrinkling to start.
Since the vertical part of the blankholder-die gap cannot vary if the tools are rigid, and
would realistically not vary much if they were modelled as elastic, considering the tool
material and dimentiosn, the step 1 blank needs to be thin enough to fit through this fixed
1.2mm gap. With the contact model used here, that upper limit was that all elements
needed to be t < 1.25mm. While this might not seem very accurate, the scans show the
same step 1 thicknesses, which also fit through the step 2 tool geometry. The prevailing
hypothesis is that this is possible due to the asymmetry of the production setup and
accompanying tool deflection throughout the process.
Another important point relating to the contact is that the contact stiffness needs to
be lower for this model. As the geometries of tools, especially the blankholder, are more
advanced than step 1, they require more elements, and thus nodes, to accurately represent
the geometry. This means that the amount of nodes in contact is higher, meaning that
the contact stiffness needs to be higher. Opposing this is the amount of nodes in contact
at any time, as well as the size discrepancy in elements in contact. As the blankholder
and blank contact is on a smaller area, and since the blankholder does not have any
excessively large elements in contact like the step 1 blankholder, the stiffness needs to
be lower. Finding the correct stiffness is tricky, as contact problems will result in error
termination if it is too high or low. For the step 2 geometry the scale factors on default
slave and master penalty stiffness needs to be SFS/SFM = 2 for a 2mm blank mesh, and
SFS/SFM = 1 for a 0.5mm blank mesh.
After trial and error varying all these, a non-torn blank was achieved with a 2mm mesh.
The most important factor determined here was that the friction needed to be in a range
of µ = [0.03 −0.05], as any values outside of this resulted in some form of breakage, de-
pending on the other variables. This was transferred to step 3, but since step 4 required
a finer mesh, a model using a blank mesh size of 0.5mm was developed. Using the same
settings for the 2mm and 0.5mm blank meshes resulted in one breaking. After significant
troubleshooting, the change of SFS/SFM = 2 →1 made it possible with the finer mesh.
The springback is handled identically to after step 1, for same reasons.
The model using the 0.5mm mesh is described in Sections 4.3.8.5 and 4.3.8.12, as this is
slightly more optimized.
4.3.8.5
Final step 2 model setup
The BOUNDARY constraints are similar to step 1, with the exception of an additional
*BOUNDARY_PRESCRIBED_MOTION_RIGID for the blankholder clamping motion,
using the curve defined in Table 4. The nodal constraints of the previous step will be
transferred with the workpiece, but it is advised to delete these and use node sets as
described for step 1.
For *CONSTRAINED the same keyword *CONSTRAINED_RIGID_BODY_STOPPERS
95 of 125
==PAGE:102==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
is used, with a similar configuration, except for the upper and lower limits, being defined
as ±0.8mm using curves. While the gap does not displace beyond ±0.3mm for most of
the process, when the much thicker edge elements move through the gap, element locking
will occur if it is not allowed to increase significantly.
For CONTACTs, the setup is the same as for step 1, with the exception to the lowered
friction FS = 0.05, and lowered penalty stiffness SFS/SFM = 1. Due to the clamping
motion, the birth time of the blankholder-blank contact is set to BT = 0.1.
All CONTROL keywords are identical, with the exception of the termination time being
increased to ENDTIM = 6.5 to allow for the initial clamping motion. The clamping takes
1s, so the process time is effectively decreased by 0.5s. This decrease is due to the thick
edge elements, as they would tear if they are allowed to enter the punch-blankholder gap,
meaning the process has to be stopped just before this occurs, in order to prevent error
termination. The important aspect is that LS-DYNA will not output the final workpiece
if an error termination is reached instead of a normal termination.
All DATABASE keywords are identical, with only the output part id changed, as im-
porting a previous part causes the part id to change.
The amount of curves are similar, using the new process parameters, with one added
for the clamping motion as described above. The only change which has not been de-
scribed yet is the velocity limit, starting at 1mm
s , staying there until t = 2s, and then
gradually increasing to 5mm
s . The low start is to prevent contact issues, as in step 1, and
the increase is to allow for the changes needed when much thicker edge of the workpiece
enters through the gap near the end of the process. If it is not allowed to change more,
as in quickly increase the gap, then the edge will lock and tear.
A new set of keywords in the INTERFACE category is added automatically. This defines
the initial stress and strains of the elements, and is imported with the "dynain" file con-
taining the workpiece of the previous step.
The load, material, and section, keywords are identical to those of step 1.
The only
change beyond this is that the *PART_PART keywords need to be updated.

Final step 2 simulation results
The final resulting geometry of step 2 is presented here.
(a)
(b)
Figure 55: (a) Scan results for reference. (b) Final step 2 blank geometry, showing the
resulting edge thickness after last step.
Starting with a thickness map, which can be observed on Figure 55. The thickness range
of [0.923mm, 1.409mm] does not fit that of the scans, [0.82mm, 1.33mm], very well.
This is mainly due to the high tool deflection asymmetry, but could also be the low
simulation friction decreasing the amount of stretching. The general thickness contours
97 of 125
==PAGE:104==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
approximately match the scans, showing regions of increased thickness around the corner
of the punch, and near the short radius. In the scans, the edge thickness is fairly uniform
at 1.28mm −1.33mm, where as the simulation edge thickness varies between ≈1.2mm
and ≈1.4mm. The cause of this discrepancy can clearly be seen on the simulation result
edge, being wrinkling near the end, caused by the large gap increase near the end. Aver-
aging the thicknesses in the regions along the wall height, the thicknesses do not deviate
significantly. The flat surface thickness on the scans are approximately 1mm, where as
the simulations show thickness very slightly below that, at 0.97mm −1.00mm. With the
asymmetry of the production part, such deviations is to be expected. The region of de-
creased thickness near the long radius is present in both the scans and simulation. On the
scans this region differs between the sides, being approximately 0.85mm on one side and
0.98mm on the other. Considering that it is approximately 0.93mm in the simulation, it
seems to accurately simulate the production part if the process was completely symmetric.
In conclusion the simulation and production part thicknesses match fairly well, with the
largest deviation being the thickness concentrations in the edge wrinkles.
Figure 56: Blankholder displacement represented as gap size throughout the process for
the final model.
Observing Figure 56, the gap quickly decreases to the maximum thickness of the workpiece
region. As previously explained, this start at t = 1s, as the clamping motion increases
the gap to 11.2mm, and then clamps down gradually to the CAD thickness of 1.2mm,
ending at t = 1s. The gap then gradually increases as the thicker regions of the workpiece
is pulled through the gap. As the thickness change along the wall length is not linear, and
as the radial stress changes the thickness, line is jagged. At the force decrease point, the
force is not sufficient to keep the gap equal to the highest thickness. While increasing the
force would be a logical step, increasing the blankholder force causes pressure concentra-
tions on the edge thickness concentrations, causing locking and thus tearing of the blank.
as previously described, if the gap is not allowed to increase this much, the thickest edge
region will not fit through the gap, causing it to break.

==PAGE:105==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
It should here be noted that the blankholder gap represented on the Figure 56 is based
on the vertical y-axis displacement of the blankholder, offset by the initial gap. Since
the actual gap is slightly angled varying across the geometry, the actual values beyond
the initial might vary very slightly, but these variations would be so small as to not have
visible effect on the graph.
Figure 57: Final step 2 blank geometry, showing the resulting effective strain.
As can be observed from Figure 57, the general strain change of this process is lower than
that of step 1, which is to be expected, as this is re-drawing, and step 1 was deep-drawing.
The only exception to this is the wrinkle lines near the edge, where the relative increase
is very drastic. This increase is lower for the coarser meshes, as, for example, the largest
attempted 2mm mesh workpiece does not show any wrinkling. It should here be noted
that this should be including the initial strains from the previous process. For later steps,
this however contrasts with it being defined as a monotonically increasing scalar, but it
is presumed that this is a problem related to the automatic colour scale.

4.3.8.7
Step 3
For the third step, initially the exact same model was used as for step 2, with the new
tools, and new forces and movements corresponding to the process. Since step 3 is in a
new press together with step 4, 5, and 6, the correct blankholder pressure is not known.
Since only step 3 and 4 have blankholders, the total blankholder force according to the
production is 1000kN throughout the process, presumably equally split between the two
processes, making it 500kN per process, divided by 4 for the quarter model.
One major change from the previous steps is that the production CAD gap between
the blankholder and die is decreased to a uniform 1mm, and the vertical gap between
punch and blankholder is no longer uniform, varying from 1.2mm, at the long and short
radius, to 1.35mm at the punch corners. The blankholder-die gap also no longer extends
over the die rounding, eliminating the minimum unchangeable radial gap of step 2, rela-
tively significantly simplifying the simulation process.
101 of 125
==PAGE:108==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
As expected based on the experience from step 1, using a fixed gap results in almost
instant tearing of the workpiece, and increasing it results in almost instant wrinkling and
element locking. A fixed blankholder-die gap of 1mm also makes no sense in relation to
the scan thicknesses. For step 2 this could be explained by the tool deflection, but this is
not the case for step 3, as explained in Section 4.1. The gap was therefore changed to a
constrained force controlled gap, as the step 2 model. As using the prescribed force results
in instant tearing, just as step 1, the same example was followed by dividing the force by
10, resulting in 50kN per process, reduced to 12.5kN for the simulation. However, run-
ning with this force for step 3, results in the gap almost instantly increasing to its upper
limit. As this essentially makes it a fixed gap, this force was not applicable. Running sev-
eral similar models with differing forces resulted in an optimal force of 15kN. Increasing
beyond this point results in locking, and subsequently tearing, at different stages of the
process, depending on the magnitude of the increase.
While a configuration which resulted on no tearing could not be attained at this point,
the simulations started stopping at seemingly random points. After testing all contact
options, several constraint and movement settings for the punch and blankholder, it was
concluded that this was not the cause. Assuming that the tool meshes and blank mesh
was somehow incompatible, the tool meshes were significantly refined and the geometry
checked. The final resulting tool geometry is shown on Figure 60.
102 of 125
==PAGE:109==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
Figure 60: Step 3 simulation setup showing the tools.
After significant troubleshooting, the reason for the seemingly random stops was due to
changing the model input/output directory. While it does not seem important, the effort
and time required for the troubleshooting is very important from the practical perspective.
Since significant output is needed for properly studying and understanding what happens
during the process, an output interval of 0.1s is used. This results in output in the range
of 4 −16Gb. With a multi-step process requiring this many intermediate simulations for
troubleshooting, storage space becomes an issue. Apparently the new network drive used
for step 3 was not suitable. If LS-DYNA cannot access the output directory whenever it
finishes a new intermediate "d3plot" file, the simulation terminates with no indication of
the problem. As a new d3plot file is produced periodically depending on the simulation,
103 of 125
==PAGE:110==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
any disconnect can result in failure, if it overlaps with one such output. If the simulation
already tends to error terminate for other reasons, diagnosing this can be very difficult.
With these problems solved, the process consistently ran without problems until the edge
of the workpiece entered the gap. Here it would get stuck in various ways depending on
the settings. Two common failure mechanisms are shown in Figure 61.
(a) Common failure mechanism 1.
(b) Common failure mechanism 2.
Figure 61: Example of common failure mechanisms for step 3.
These failures manifest in a similar manner to step 2. That said, many combinations of
variables can cause either of the results shown on Figure 61.
Failure mechanism 1, Figure 61a, is common if the pressure throughout the process has
not been sufficiently high to limit thickening, often by setting the friction too high, in
relation to counteracting the blankholder force, causing some elements to become thicker
than the upper limit of the blankholder movement, or thick enough that they are sub-
jected to the full blankholder force.
Failure mechanism 2, Figure 61b, either tearing as the edge tries to enter the gap, or
starting to tear along the curved surface of the blankholder, is common if the pressure at
the end is too high, often either from too low friction increasing the effective blankholder
force, or the gap upper limit being set too low. As the thickness in the corner tends
to be higher than the rest of the flange, the pressure concentrates on fewer elements.
This causes the friction force to drop, increasing the blankholder pressure and locking the
flange. Figure 61b is also commonly how it looks if the gap is allowed to increase too
much, creating wrinkles, extending out into folds outside the gap, which can then not
enter the gap.
The configuration which first resulted in no tearing or error termination was to set the
friction to µ = 0.05, and the blankholder gap constraints to ±0.3 for 1s −4s, and then
increasing to ±0.6 for 4s −6.5s, linearly increasing between the two limits. Furthermore,
104 of 125
==PAGE:111==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
the velocity constraint of the blankholder needs to start at 1mm
s
or lower for contact prob-
lems, and then it needs to increase to 5mm
s
at 4s. Using a friction coefficient above or
below this results in breakage. Allowing more blankholder movement at the first half will
decrease the gap too much, locking the workpiece in the gap, and if it is not allowed to
increase drastically towards the end, then thick elements will get stuck either in the gap,
or trying to enter it. The result of this using the 2mm mesh workpiece can be seen on
Figure 62.
Figure 62: Resulting blank geometry of step 3, using a 2mm workpiece mesh.
Observing Figure 62, the scale shown very high thickness, but the higher values are concen-
trated in a single element on the edge. For unknown reasons this element did not conflict
with the 1.35mm gap. It is clear that the workpiece has not completely entered the gap.
This is due to the excessive wall length near the long radius being carried over from step 1.
Attempting to get the same result with a 0.5mm mesh, resulted in several error ter-
minations.
After extensive testing, this model can run with a finer mesh, but only
if SFS/SFM = 2, and if BSORT ≤2 for the blankholder-blank contact, and if if
BSORT ≤5 for the other two contacts. The small areas, many small curved surfaces,
and high node counts, causes the model to be very prone to contact problems, which is why
the bucket sort frequency needs to be very low. This is explored further in Section 4.3.4.4.
As the very localized and extreme thickening seen in Figure 62 is spread out to more
than one node for the finer mesh, it can no longer enter the punch-blankholder gap, and
needs to be terminated before this occurs to prevent an error termination. This changes
the termination time from ENDTIM = 7s to ENDTIM = 6.6s, and means that a signifi-
cant flange remains.

processing errors. Excluding the flange near the long radius, resulting from step 1, the

Figure 64: Blankholder displacement represented as gap size throughout the process for
the final model.
Observing Figure 64, the clamping motion is here excluded as for step 2. The gap increases
steadily from the set gap of 1.2mm until it reaches its first upper limit. This increase it
not due to the actual thickness of the section passing through the gap at that time, but
the friction force being sufficiently high to open the gap. While this could be considered
undesirable, using a friction coefficient below µ = 0.05 is not likely to be realistic for
lubricated surfaces between aluminium and tool-steel[37][38], and would result in tearing
at other stages of the process. It stays at the upper limit until the limit is increased, and
the increase between the limits is linear due to the velocity constraint. If it is allowed to
increase faster, contact issues occur. While this movement is very artificial, as previously
described, changing any of the parameters which could remedy this results in part breakage
or error termination.

4.3.8.10
Step 4
Using the model from step 3, the step 4 process was simulated. The tool geometry was
re-meshed several times, but the final tool geometry is included in Figure 68.
111 of 125
==PAGE:118==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
Figure 68: Final step 4 tool geometry.
The setup and values of step 3 resulted in almost instant error termination.
As the
tool geometry requires more nodes, with less area in the initial contacts, this was due to
contact errors. Lowering the BSORT to 2 or 1 for all contacts allowed it to move for-
ward. Lowering the stiffness scaling from SFS/SFM = 2 also increases contact stability,
with SFS/SFM = 1.5 being the most stable. However, the difference in simulation time
between BSORT = 1 and BSORT = 2 is negligible, and using BSORT = 1 allows for
SFS/SFM = [0.5, 2] without problems.
While the process could start, it stops at t = 3.7s if no errors occur before then. No
errors are apparent, and it does not result in error termination, but rather stays at ≈48%
"running" until manually terminated.
As the steps progressed, this type of failure became more common, with the step 3 and
4 simulations almost exclusively stopping at some point of the simulation without au-
tomatically error terminating. The cause for this is unknown, but does not seem to be
connected to any process parameters, keyword settings, or meshes used. This occurs no
112 of 125
==PAGE:119==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
matter if the simulations has failed due to tearing, element locking, or contact instability.
While it would be reasonable to assume that the simulations are still running, just at
automatically significantly lower time-steps, turning off adaptive time-step control, and
setting a lower limit on the time-step size through the *CONTROL_TERMINATION
and *CONTROL_TIMESTEP does not resolve this issue.
While many variables can cause the process to break before t = 3.7s, none are presently
able to go beyond this. The attempted solutions are listed here,
1. Varying force in the range of [2kN, 30kN]. Most of these result in breakage, but the
curve described in Section 4.3.8.11 will progress to t = 3.7s.
2. Varying force during the process, and varying the force as a function of blankholder
displacement.
3. Varying mass scaling in the range [-1e-4, -1e-5]. Here -3e-5 gives the best results,
with values below -1-5 exceeding 3 days simulation time.
4. Varying the time-step control between automatic, manual, and curve defined.
5. Varying blankholder gap displacement limits. To prevent the possibility of locking
these are set at ±2mm in the final model.
6. Varying blankholder gap displacement limits during the process, like in step 2.
7. Varying the blankholder velocity limit. As for previous models, a limit greater than
5mm
s
is likely to cause contact issues, where as limits below 1mm
s
is likely to cause
locking.
8. Varying the blankholder velocity limit during the process, like in step 2.
9. Varying the initial gap size.
10. Varying the tool mesh, its size, configuration, refinement of curves, and corner
geometries. The corner geometries refers to preventing contact with sharp edges. A
total of 6 tool mesh configurations were attempted.
11. Varying the friction values in the range of µ = [0.0, 0.5], including varying it between
individual contacts. Getting to t = 3.7s without breakage is most likely for µ = 0.05
in all contacts.
12. Varying the viscous damping coefficient in the range of VDC = [0, 80].
13. Varying the scale factors on penalty stiffness in the range of SFS/SFM = [0.1, 3],
and varying it between contacts, and using different values for slave and master
scaling. SFS/SFM = [0.5, 2] works best, with SFS/SFM = 2 having the best chance
of reaching t = 3.7s.
14. Varying the search depth in the range DEPTH = [1, 5] in the contact keywords.
Here DEPTH = 1 is the most stable, but at the cost of increased simulation time.
15. Varying bucket sort frequency in the range BSORT = [1, 5]. Here a minimum of
BSORT = 2 is required for all contacts, with BSORT = 1 being the most likely to
result in reaching t = 3.7s.
113 of 125
==PAGE:120==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
16. Varying the tool thicknesses, which here essentially varies the allowed penetration
and relative checking depth. 1mm is found to be the optimal setting.
17. Varying the hourglass coefficient. Changes small enough to not fail immediately
had no other effect.
18. Rerunning step 1-4 using a four times finer mesh at 0.5mm, and retrying several of
the changes on this new mesh.
19. Using adaptive meshing at various settings for ADPFREQ, ADPTOL, ADPOPT,
MAXLVL, and ADPSIZE, at various stages of the simulation.
20. Using different amounts and frequencies of output.
21. Using different types of storage devices.
22. Using computers to run the simulations.
23. Increasing the allocated memory for the simulation.
24. Varying the amount of CPU cores used.
25. Varying the LS-DYNA version and licence.
26. Manually terminating and restarting the simulations.
27. Simplifying the keyword file using a text-editor.
As a side-note, varying the force as a function is done using *DEFINE_CURVE_FUNCTION
instead of *DEFINE_CURVE. The setting used here is DY (29190) ∗100000 + 12500.
"DY()" defines the y-axis displacement of the given node, here being a node on the
blankholder. As the displacement is in millimeters, it is set to be 12500N at zero dis-
placement, and then lowering with smaller gap and increasing for a larger gap.
This
setting is not realistic for the production setup, but can be used to prevent sudden veloc-
ity changes and improve contact stability.
As none of these have any effect on the ability to proceed beyond t = 3.7s, the only
two conclusions are that either some limit related to the blank transfer has been reached,
or that some part of the production geometry does not function with one of the keywords
used.
As this project does have a time-constraint, the most stable and simple model reach-
ing t = 3.7s is presented here, along with its results.
114 of 125
==PAGE:121==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing

Final step 4 simulation results
The final resulting geometry of step 4 is presented here.

through the gap, the thicknesses and contours match those of the scan almost exactly.
