Figure 70: Blankholder displacement represented as gap size throughout the process for
the final model.
Again not much can be seen on Figure 70, but interestingly it manages to increase fairly
significantly despite the aggressive force increase defined by the function. At the dis-
placement shown for t = 3.5s and beyond, the blankholder pressure has increased to
16kN.
Figure 71: Final step 4 blank geometry, showing the resulting effective strain.
117 of 125
==PAGE:124==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
As can be observed from Figure 71, the strain follows the same pattern as for step 3,
with the exception of the punch corner wall, which encounters significant increases at an
earlier stage of the process.
Figure 72: Step 4 at the end of the simulation, showing the resulting effective stress (Von
Mises).
Figure 72 clearly shows the stress increasing very heavily for some nodes near the corner.
This indicates elements locking. While other steps have shown similar patterns of some
nodes seeing much higher values at intermediate stages of the process, none have seen
such high values. The notion of it actually being nodes locking, is however countered
by the same pattern occurring at the same time for differing tool and blank meshes, as
well as when using adaptive meshing to lower the element size in the relevant location to
below 0.1mm. This could indicate that the elements need to be below that size for the
given tool geometry and process parameters, in order to prevent locking, but considering
that a 2mm mesh sees no such problems in step 3, which is arguably very similar, this
does not seem likely.
No springback is performed on this model, as the process does not reach completion.
4.3.9
Important additional notes
All models here are using ELEFORM = 16 fully integrated elements. From the docu-
mentation it is not very clear, but this can only change thickness based on plane stress.
Any out of plane stress such as compression will not affect the thickness. This has been
confirmed by simulating a flat surface in purely perpendicular compression. The result
of this is that the elements retain their initial thickness despite clearly being compressed
between two surfaces. It is therefore hypothesised that this is why all zero-friction models
118 of 125
==PAGE:125==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
attempted here have failed. Thus, keeping in mind that any out of plane compression will
not be accurately represented if no sliding motion is present with friction in the contacts.
As previously explained, there are element types which should be able to model this, but
for unknown reasons these have not proven applicable to the attempted models.
When saving a model, it is a good idea to reopen that saved model in a new instance
of LS-DYNA and checking the options. Furthermore, when starting a simulation, one
should keep an eye on the progress using the option to open a command prompt window
in LS-Run, for at least one iteration/time-step. Although it seems to be a rare occurrence,
sometimes keywords are removed or reset to default when saving. The keywords which
most commonly disappear are those of the *BOUNDARY category, and those which are
most commonly reset to default seem to be those in the *CONTROL category. There
does not seem to be any specific conditions which cause this, but it seems more likely to
occur when having multiple LS-DYNA instances open, or when running a model.
While it might seem trivial, when running simulations which take multiple days to finish,
it can become a major source of wasted effort. While this happens rarely, and checking
all keywords for incorrect configurations is tiresome, it is important to remember.
When simulating a model which requires multiple simulations, it is advisable to save
keyword files as new instead of overwriting the previous model. Since some keywords
might be reset when saving, if the previous options were not saved in a separate file, these
will be permanently lost. While some options can easily be remembered, defined curves
with many defined points can not.
Another important aspect to using the command prompt window feature in LS-Run,
is that all simulations will terminate when it is closed. This does not seem to be the case
for the "stop" command, which does not work in some cases. When running the simula-
tions on computers which cannot easily be restarted, such as remote or server computers,
this can lead to a situation where no new simulations can be started before the current
one terminates, or the server is reset.
Initially it was attempted to mainly control the model by manually editing the files. This
is possible, and a good alternative to the not so user-friendly user interface, for smaller
models. However, when the files include large amounts of nodes, node sets, elements, etc.
this is not practical.
LS-DYNA has a function for predicting the optimal blank geometry for a given sheet
forming process. Use of this has been attempted here. Use of this function is not in-
tuitive, especially creating the targeting curves, but it can be done without significant
training. This function however does not work for quarter models, which is why extensive
use of this has not been attempted here. Furthermore, it can only be performed for one
step at a time, and requires a fully meshed part of the desired finished geometry. It is
further limited to small changes to the blank, so an accurate guess of the initial blank
geometry is needed as well. Considering these limitations, as well as several of the steps
having significant problems in the flange regions, it is not suitable for such advanced ge-
ometries. Since this functions initial purpose is predicting the final positions of holes on
the initial blank after sheet metal forming, this outcome was expected.
119 of 125
==PAGE:126==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
5
Conclusion
All four steps relevant to this project have been simulated, with the first three simulations
resulting in reasonably accurate approximations of the resulting production geometries.
The fourth step of the process has not resulted in a finished geometry. The reason why
the fourth step cannot be complete has not been found, but it is hypothesised that either
the geometry is not suitable for simulating with the attempted model, that some critical
limit has been reached by errors accumulating throughout the steps, or that the limits of
multi-step simulation using LS-DYNA have been reached.
It has been found that not all process parameters are suitable for the types of simulations
attempted here. The most prominent of these being modelling a fixed gap blankholder,
which is not suitable for this type of simulation.
In developing the models and refining them until accurate results could be reached, a
multitude of difficulties have been discovered. In regards to the process in question, so-
lutions have been found to most of these. Through the process of finding solutions to
problems for each steps, methodologies have been developed to facilitate troubleshooting
in modelling of other similar processes. As most of the difficulties apply to several or all
of the steps, it is probable that the solutions and their methodologies will apply to sim-
ulation efforts of other multi-step deep-drawing and redrawing processes of non-circular
geometries.
Regarding whether this type of simulation would have been a suitable tool during the
run-in of this process, the overall assessment here is, that this is not suitable without
significant prior experience using LS-DYNA to simulate similar processes, and without a
suitable simulation environment, mostly meaning significant available computing power.
There are however several aspects to whether this is a worthwhile endeavour.
By constantly running simulations, and often multiple at a time, and constantly working
on figuring out why simulations failed and how these problems could be resolved, for
a period of five months, it was only possible to fully simulate three out of four steps.
Furthermore, this was only possible by significantly deviating from the true process pa-
rameters. While this is a very significant amount of effort and time required, comparing
it to the length of the run-in of the process, being approximately half a year, this is
not critical. The required time could also be significantly reduced. Firstly, this time is
relative to the experience of the user, and the available equipment. This process was
performed under the conditions of initially very limited experience using LS-DYNA, very
limited initial theoretical FEM background, and very limited experience using other FEA
programs. Furthermore, it was performed with very limited available computing power,
as the licences were limited to relatively weak computing hardware, and limited to a
maximum of 4 CPU cores for each simulation. The process could also feasibly become
significantly faster, if the people making the simulation models have direct access to the
production setup and intricate knowledge of the machines and processes. As much is
lost in communication with large physical distance between production and simulation,
having both in-house and performed by the same people, would realistically significantly
facilitate the process. As removing some or all of these obstacles would significantly fa-
cilitate the simulation process, implementing LS-DYNA simulations for facilitating the
120 of 125
==PAGE:127==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
run-in process would become a worthwhile endeavour.
The main reason for why it is here only recommended if several of the mentioned obsta-
cles are not present, is that the user experience of working with simulating this process,
for the extended periods of time required here, has not been good. Expending multiple
days, weeks, and months, trying to troubleshoot a model, constantly looking at the same
model, coming in every day to error messages and halfway finished simulations, just to
discover, for example, that some part of the initial model was altered during saving, or
that a specific model will only work under very specific process conditions for often in-
explicable reasons, is extremely demoralizing. This is further compounded by the most
efficient workflow not being compatible with a normal or healthy work-schedule. As the
amount of intermediate simulations, meaning those which either error terminate or does
not provide a desirable final outcome, far outweighs the amount of final simulations for
a process, to ensure optimal utilization of the available hardware and software, the user
needs to be constantly checking the status of running simulations, in order to start new
simulations as quickly as possible. As the built-in features for estimating simulation time,
queuing simulations, and restarting simulations, has been found to be completely useless
for these types of models, any sort of useful scheduling is not feasible.
As many of the features and geometries of these specific processes pose significant problems
for using LS-DYNA to simulate them, the recommendation here is to build up experience
by applying this tool to simpler and more suitable processes, before attempting to simu-
late multi-stage re- and deepdrawing of parts with rectangular features. This especially
relates to refraining from initially attempting to simulate on poorly understood processes,
in relation to for example unknown exact tool movement and positions, press settings,
possible tool deflection, friction parameters, and material parameters, such as degree of
anisotropy and stress-strain curves.
121 of 125
==PAGE:128==
August 5, 2022
Simulation of multi-stage deep drawing
6
Future work
The most prevalent next step would be to examine what is preventing the fourth step
from reaching a completed state, how it can be solved, and how this can be prevented in
the future. After reaching a finished state for step 4, the effects of the process parameters
can then be investigated in relation to the step 4 production part.
Another area of interest would be further examining the causes of the geometric devi-
ations seen in step 1, and how to prevent this in future simulations. Assuming that the
cause is non-uniform friction, developing a model which could accurately model this would
be desirable.
With the final geometry, further investigation could be performed on the blank geom-
etry prediction tool.
Knowing the parameter bounds and settings which will result in finished geometries,
the effects of varying several of the parameters within these ranges would be desirable, in
order to fully understand their effects. As this would require more accurate measurements
on the production part.
Further investigation of the true production setup would also be desirable, in order to
determine how, and how significantly, the simulation process parameters differ from the
the production parameters. Since they can produce similar parts, this would shed light
on how future production parameters should be modelled to achieve accurate results.

DIN 50125:2009-07
Foreword
This standard has been prepared by Working Committee NA 062-01-42 AA “Zug- und Duktilitätsprüfung für
Metalle” of the Normenausschuss Materialprüfung (Materials Testing Standards Committee).
DIN EN ISO 6892-1 lays down general specifications regarding tensile test piece shapes. More detailed
examples of tensile test piece shapes may be given in product standards.
For this reason Working Committee NA 062-01-42 AA felt it was important that DIN 50125 (which applies to
tensile test piece shapes in Germany) remains valid to facilitate the manufacturing of such test pieces in the
factory or workshop. This edition of the standard specifies examples of tensile test piece shapes which meet
the overall conditions specified in DIN EN ISO 6892-1.
Amendments
The following amendments have been made to DIN 50125:2004-01:
a)
Tolerances on dimensions and shape have been harmonized with DIN EN ISO 6892-1.
b)
Symbols have been harmonized with DIN EN ISO 6892-1.
c)
The transition radius, parallel length, and total length of test pieces have been harmonized with
DIN EN ISO 6892-1.
Previous editions
DIN DVM 125 = DIN 50125: 1940-08, 1951-04, 1986-03, 1991-04, 2004-01
DIN 50125-1 Corrigendum 1: 2004-07
3
==PAGE:138==
DIN 50125:2009-07
1
Scope
This standard lays down specifications for test pieces which can be used for tensile testing metallic materials
in accordance with DIN EN ISO 6892-1.
The standard serves to facilitate the manufacture of tensile test pieces in the factory or workshop.
NOTE
The tensile test pieces specified here are examples of test pieces which meet the standard conditions laid
down in DIN EN ISO 6892-1. Test pieces which deviate from the examples given here, but which meet the requirements of
DIN EN ISO 6892-1, are also deemed to be in compliance with this standard.
2
Normative references
The following referenced documents are indispensable for the application of this document. For dated
references, only the edition cited applies. For undated references, the latest edition of the referenced
document (including any amendments) applies.
DIN EN ISO 6892-1, Metallic materials — Tensile testing — Part 1: Method of test at room temperature
3
Terms and definitions
For the purposes of this standard the terms and definitions in DIN EN ISO 6892-1 apply.
4
Types, dimensions, and designation
4.1
General
The tolerances specified in Table 1 shall apply to test pieces as specified in 4.2 to 4.6 and 4.9.
4

DIN 50125:2009-07
NOTE 1
Flat test pieces are usually to be taken from strip, sheet, bars and sections. The edges should be deburred. If
the rolling skin is not machined off, the test piece thickness
o is equal to the product thickness.
NOTE 2
Where flat test pieces are machined on all surfaces, the specified surface roughness applies for all surfaces,
with the exception of the test piece ends.

NOTE 1
Flat test pieces are usually to be taken from strip and sheet. The edges should be deburred. Where possible,
the rolling skin should not be machined off.
NOTE 2
Manufacturing test pieces by punching may result in significant changes in the material characteristics,
particularly the proof stress (due to strain hardening). For materials which are known to strain-harden, test pieces should
be prepared by milling, grinding, etc.
5
Marking
Tensile test pieces shall be marked in such a way that, after testing, it is still possible to determine from which
sample the test pieces were taken, as well as their location and direction in the sample, where necessary. The
marking shall be applied to the face or side of one end, or to both ends, where necessary.
